Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the method of perfection is sufficient on its own without reference to power.
Correction: The commentator shows that the method of perfection ultimately relies on the method of power because perfection alone does not imply knowledge unless the agent acts with intention and choice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the power method considered stronger?
A) Because it relies on fewer premises.
B) Because the perfection method faces a difficult question about an intermediary.
C) Because it is more intuitive.
D) Because it avoids discussing perfection.

Key Takeaway
The method of power is more robust because the method of perfection requires additional assumptions about intentional agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order and precision in your life, do you attribute it to your own planning or to Allah's intentional power? What would change if you genuinely saw every 'perfect' outcome as dependent on His choice, not just your effort?
?
This lesson shows that outward perfection (like good deeds) does not automatically imply a good intention behind them. Examine a recent act of worship or kindness: was it done with conscious intentionality for Allah, or out of habit? What step will you take today to purify your intention?
?
The passage warns against assuming that mere necessity (without choice) can produce knowledge or perfection. Where in your life have you assumed that a good result automatically means you are on the right path, without examining your motives? Will you commit to asking yourself before any action: 'Is this from my own compulsion or from my free choice for Allah?'

Clarifications on Power Scope and the Falling Away
Three clarifications: scope of power, condition of perfection, and the falling away of the sleeping objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scope of power (over little and much) does not affect the entailment of will for knowledge, and why the condition of perfection and the concept of heedlessness relate to the objections.

Core Idea
Power covers all quantities; will entails knowledge necessarily.

This lesson addresses three clarifications: first, whether the scope of power (over little or much) affects the objection; second, the addition of 'perfect' to the act to show the question applies to the first method as well; and third, why the sleeping objection falls away only for those who consider sleep opposite to power, but heedlessness may still be an issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarifications passage
Read the Arabic passage which contains three distinct points: the scope of power, the condition of perfection, and the falling away.
Each point begins with 'قوله:'
2
Understand the scope of power
The objector says the definition of power covers both small and large acts, so why is there a specific question? The answer: the question is an objection, and the entailment of will for knowledge is necessary regardless of quantity.
The commentator refers to his earlier work.
3
Analyze the condition of perfection
The commentator notes that adding the condition 'perfect' to the act shows that the objection applies also to the first method (presumably the method of perfection itself).
4
Examine the falling away
The phrase 'falling away' refers to the question by the sleeping and heedless. If sleep is opposite to power, the question drops, but if the person is heedless (not opposite), the question might remain.
The commentator suggests the author may intend to retain the question for the heedless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The condition of perfection (itqan) is added to the act. Do you settle for imperfect acts in your worship, or do you strive for mastery as a sign of true power? What is one specific area where you will raise your standard of itqan this week?

The First Proof: God's Knowledge from His Incorporeality
First proof for God's knowledge from incorporeality, with objection to its major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for God's knowledge based on His incorporeality and the objection that challenges its major premise.

Core Idea
If God is incorporeal, He must know all universals — but can that premise be proven?

After establishing foundational proofs, the text presents an additional way: God is incorporeal, and every incorporeal knows all universals. This lesson unpacks that argument and the crucial objection that its major premise has already been shown weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic proof and its objection carefully. Note that the objection targets the major premise.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof relies on (1) God is incorporeal, (2) every incorporeal knows all universals. Ask yourself: Are these established?
The author claims the premises were proven earlier.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says the proof of the major premise (that every incorporeal knows all universals) was already shown weak. What does this imply for the argument?
This seriously undermines the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثبات علمه تعالى أيضاً مسلكان: المسلك الأول أنه مجرد أي ليس جسماً ولا جسمانياً كما مر في التنزيهات، وكل مجرد فهو عاقل لجميع الكليات وقد برهنا فيما سلف على المقدمتين. المسلك الثاني: أنه تعالى يعقل ذاته وإذا عقل ذاته عقل ما عداه، أما الأول فلأن التعقل حضور الماهية المجردة عن العلائق المادية للشيء المجرد القائم بذاته، وهو حاصل في شأنه لأن ذاته مجردة غير غائبة عن ذاته فيكون عالماً بذاته. وأما الثاني: فلانه مبدأ لما سواه أي لجميعه إما بواسطة أو بدونها، والعلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول، فيكون عالماً بذاته وبجميع معلولاته. ويرد على المسلك الأول منهم الكبرى القائلة: بأن كل مجرد عاقل للمفهومات الكلية، وبرهانه الذي تمسكوا به قد مر ضعفه. ويرد على المسلك الثاني: لا نسلم أن التعقل ما ذكرتم، وتعريفه بذلك لا يوجب الجزم بأن حقيقته ذلك ما لم يقم عليه برهان، إذ غايته أنهم يعنون بالتعقل ذلك المعنى الذي عرفوه به، ولكن من أين لهم أن الحالة التي نجدها نحن من أنفسنا ونسميها العلم حقيقته ذلك الذي ذكروه، لا بد له من دليل. سلمناه، أي سلمنا أن حقيقة العلم ما ذكرتموه، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يشترط فيه التغاير بين الحاضر وما حضر هو عنده فلا يكون الشيء عالما بنفسه.
Proof of His knowledge, exalted, also has two ways: The first way: that He is incorporeal, i.e., not a body nor corporeal as previously mentioned in the transcendences, and every incorporeal is intellective of all universals, and we have already proved the two premises. The second way: that He, exalted, intellects His essence, and if He intellects His essence, He intellects what is other than it. As for the first, because intellection is the presence of the incorporeal essence, free from material relations, to the incorporeal thing subsisting by itself. And that obtains in His case because His essence is incorporeal, not absent from itself, so He is knowledgeable of His essence. As for the second, because He is the origin of what is other than Him, i.e., all of it, either through an intermediary or without it, and knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, so He is knowledgeable of His essence and of all His effects. And there is objected to the first way from them: the major premise which says that every incorporeal is intellective of universal concepts, and its proof which they adopted has already been shown to be weak. And there is objected to the second way: we do not accept that intellection is what you mentioned, and defining it as such does not necessitate certainty that its reality is that unless a proof is established for it. For the utmost is that they mean by intellection that meaning which they defined it by, but how do they know that the state which we find in ourselves and call knowledge is that reality which they mentioned? That requires a proof. We concede that, i.e., we concede that the reality of knowledge is what you mentioned, but why is it not permissible that distinction between the presence and that before which it is present be a condition, so that a thing is not knowledgeable of itself?
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents two additional proofs for God's knowledge and then critiques both. The first way relies on God's incorporeality: since He is not a body or corporeal, and every incorporeal being is intellective of all universals, God knows all universals. The two premises (incorporeality of God and the link between incorporeality and intellection) are claimed to be proved earlier. The second way argues that God intellects His essence, and by knowing His essence as the cause, He knows all effects. The explanation: intellection is the presence of an incorporeal essence to itself, and God's essence is present to itself; also, as the cause of all things, knowledge of the cause gives knowledge of the effect. Then objections are raised: for the first way, the major premise (all incorporeals know all universals) is rejected as its proof has been shown weak. For the second way, the definition of intellection is not accepted as established; it is merely a conventional definition, and the actual nature of knowledge may require a distinction between knower and known (i.e., duality), so a thing cannot know itself. This undermines the self-knowledge premise.

عاقل
كليات
مفهومات كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from incorporeality is conclusive and unassailable.
Correction: The objector challenges the major premise linking incorporeality to knowledge of all universals, noting its proof was weak, so the argument is not conclusive.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection to the first proof?
A) God is not truly incorporeal.
B) The major premise that every incorporeal knows all universals has weak proof.
C) The minor premise that God is incorporeal is false.
D) The proof relies on an invalid syllogism.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for God's knowledge from incorporeality is challenged by the weakness of its major premise, leaving the argument unconvincing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof relies on the claim that every incorporeal knows all universals, yet its proof was insufficient. Are you holding any belief about God that you have not deeply examined, simply because it seemed logical? Will you scrutinize it today?
?
This lesson shows that even a carefully constructed argument for God's attributes can be incomplete. When your reasoning reaches a dead end, do you humbly turn to revelation and ask for guidance, or do you persist in trying to force a conclusion?
?
The objection does not deny that God knows all things; it only says the proof is weak. Is your trust in God's attributes based on rational proofs alone, or on the certainty of divine revelation? What will you do to strengthen your certainty regarding God's knowledge?

The Second Proof: God's Knowledge from Self-Knowledge
Second proof for God's knowledge from self-knowledge and causality, with objections to definition and self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for God's knowledge based on His self-knowledge and the objection that questions the definition of intellection and the possibility of self-knowledge.

Core Idea
If God knows Himself as the cause, He knows all effects — but can a thing know itself?

The second proof argues that God intellects His essence, and since He is the cause of all things, knowledge of the cause yields knowledge of all effects. However, objections challenge both the definition of intellection and the very possibility of self-knowledge. This lesson examines the argument and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic proof and its detailed objection. Notice how the objection has two stages: first rejecting the definition of intellection, then even if accepted, questioning self-knowledge.
2
Break down the proof
The proof: God intellects His essence (because His essence is present to itself). Then, as the cause, knowing Himself gives knowledge of all effects. Identify these two parts.
3
Analyze the first objection
The objector says the definition of intellection as 'presence of an incorporeal essence' is not proven; it's just a convention. The real nature of knowledge may differ.
4
Analyze the second objection
Even if the definition is accepted, the objector says: why must intellection not require a distinction (taghayur) between the presence and the one to whom it is present? If so, a thing cannot know itself.
This directly attacks the premise that God knows Himself.

Objection to the First Approach: The Major Premise
Objection to the first proof's major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this objection challenges the first proof not by denying the major premise, but by discrediting its supporting argument.

Core Idea
The objection targets the proof, not the premise itself.

In theological debates, a powerful move is to show that the evidence for a claim is already refuted, rather than attacking the claim directly. Here, the objector does exactly that: he targets the major premise of the first approach by pointing out that its proof has already been shown to be weak. This lesson unpacks this concise but critical objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the objection is introduced with 'ويرد على' and then specifies the element being attacked (الكبرى).
The phrase 'قد مر ضعفه' is key—it refers to a previous discussion.
2
Identify the major premise
The major premise under attack is: 'every non-material being is cognizant of universal concepts'. Understand that this is a crucial claim in the first approach.
3
Clarify the objection's focus
The objection does not say the major premise is false, but that its proof is weak. This is a subtle but important distinction. Ask yourself: why is attacking the proof sometimes more effective than attacking the premise directly?
This objection relies on an earlier refutation that the reader is assumed to know.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider how this objection functions in the broader argument. By undermining the proof, it casts doubt on the entire first approach without having to disprove each part separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد على المسلك الأول منهم الكبرى القائلة : بأن كل مجرد عاقل للمفهومات الكلية وبرهانه الذي تمسكوا به قد مر ضعفه
And an objection is made against the first approach of them, the major premise stating that every non-material is cognizant of universal concepts, and its proof which they adhered to has already passed concerning its weakness.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This short passage presents an objection to the first approach. The objector attacks the major premise (الكبرى) of the first proof—that every non-material being is cognizant of universal concepts. He asserts that the proof used to support this premise has already been shown to be weak (قد مر ضعفه), thereby undermining the first approach.

Key Terms
كبرى
مجرد
عاقل
مفهومات كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection claims that the major premise itself is false.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'its proof has already passed concerning its weakness', so the attack is on the proof, not the premise's truth value.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to the first approach?
A) The minor premise is false.
B) The major premise is false.
C) The conclusion is false.
D) The proof for the major premise has already been shown weak.

Key Takeaway
The objection to the first approach does not deny the major premise but attacks its proof, which is claimed to have already been refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a belief about Allah that you hold strongly. What proof do you rely on for it? Is that proof sound, or have you never examined it? Write down one specific proof and assess its strength using today's lesson.

Objection to the Definition of Cognition
Initial objection to the definition of cognition and the condition of distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of cognition as mere presence is not self-evident and requires proof.

Core Idea
Definitions of cognition are not proven without demonstration.

The commentator begins by raising an objection against the claim that cognition is simply the presence of a quiddity to the mind. He argues that defining it thus does not force acceptance of its reality without a proof. This lesson unpacks that initial objection, setting the stage for deeper inquiry into the nature of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic passage where the objector says 'We do not concede that cognition is what you mentioned.'
Notice the phrase 'لا نسلم' which is a classical debating formula.
2
Understand the challenge
The objector is saying that defining cognition as presence does not prove that its reality is that; it only shows what they mean by the term.
Distinguish between definition and proof.
3
See the concession and new condition
Even if we concede the definition, the objector then asks why distinction is not also required, so that a thing cannot know itself.
This prepares for the next lesson on the condition of distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد على المسلك الثاني لا نسلم أن التعقل ما ذكرتم وتعريفه بذلك لا يوجب الجزم بأن حقيقته ذلك ما لم يقم عليه برهان إذ غايته أنهم يعنون بالتعقل ذلك المعنى الذي عرفوه به ولكن من أين لهم أن الحالة التي نجدها أنفسنا ونسميها العلم حقيقته ذلك الذي ذكروه لا بد له من دليل سلمناه أي سلمنا أن حقيقة العلم ما ذكرتموه لكن لم لا يجوز يشترط فيه التغاير بين الحاضر وما حضر هو عنده فلا يكون الشيء عالما بنفسه

قوله : (لا نسلم أن التعقل ما ذكرتم إلخ ) قيل : كيف والحضور نسبة بين الشيئين ولم يقل أحد من أن الفلاسفة بكون التعقل والعلم من المقولات النسبية، ولو جوز كون التعقل نسبة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون عبارة عن حالة نسبة أخرى يتحصل في حقنا دون بعض المجردات ؟

قوله : ( لكن لم لا يجوز أن يشترط فيه التغاير) قد يؤيد هذا بأن العلم مما يفهم بالضرورة كل أحد إما بكنهه أو بما يميز عن سائر أغياره، ونحن نعلم قطعا أن مجرد عدم غيبية الشيء عن نفسه الذي سموه بالحضور عند نفسه سواء كان مجرداً أو مادياً ليس مما يصدق عليه هذا المفهوم بل ربما يدعي أن عدم غيبية الشيء عن نفسه ليس فيه تفاوت بين المجرد وغيره، بحيث يكون أحدهما علماً والآخر غير علم قيل : المفهوم من تقرير الشارح أنه حمل هذا الكلام على أن التغاير شرط بعد تحقق الحضور ولا وجه له لأنه يؤدي إلى أن يوجد حقيقة الشيء بدونه لانتفاء شرطه وهو غير معقول. فالصواب أن مراد المصنف لم لا يجوز أن يشترط في حضور الشيء للشيء المغايرة الذاتية بين الحاضر وما حضر عنده حتى لا يتعلق علم الشيء بنفسه لانتفاء الحضور المستلزم لتلك المغايرة لا لانتفائها مع تحقق الحضور الذي هو حقيقة العلم على الفرض وأجيب بأن الإضافة في قول الشارح حقيقة العلم ما ذكرتموه مثل الإضافة في قولنا : حقيقة الهندي الإنسان لا مثلها في قولنا : حقيقة الإنسان الحيوان الناطق، وحينئذ يكون العلم حصة من حقيقة الحضور مشروطاً بالمغايرة الذاتية فلا يلزم من وجود الحضور وجود العلم كما لا يلزم من وجود الإنسان وجود الهندي وإن كان حقيقته ذلك .
And an objection is made against the second approach: We do not concede that cognition is what you mentioned, and defining it thus does not necessitate certainty that its reality is that, unless a proof is established for it; for the utmost is that they mean by cognition that meaning which they defined, but whence have they that the state which we find in ourselves and call knowledge its reality is that which they mentioned? It needs a proof. We concede it, i.e., we concede that the reality of knowledge is what you mentioned, but why may it not be that distinction between the present and that at which it is present is required in it, so that a thing is not knowing of itself?

His saying: (We do not concede that cognition is what you mentioned, etc.) It is said: How (can that be) while presence is a relation between two things, and none of the philosophers say that cognition and knowledge are from the relative categories? And if it is allowed that cognition is a relation, then why may it not be an expression of another relational state that occurs in us but not in some abstract beings?

His saying: (But why may it not be that distinction is required in it?) This may be supported by the fact that knowledge is understood necessarily by everyone, either through its essence or through what distinguishes it from other things; and we know with certainty that mere absence of a thing's being absent from itself, which they call presence to itself, whether it be abstract or material, is not something to which this concept applies; rather, it might be claimed that the absence of a thing's being absent from itself has no difference between abstract and other, such that one is knowledge and the other is not knowledge. It is said: What is understood from the commentator's exposition is that he carried this speech on the view that distinction is a condition after the realization of presence, and there is no sense to it because it leads to the existence of the reality of the thing without it due to the negation of its condition, and that is unintelligible. So the correct view is that the author meant: why may it not be required in the presence of a thing to a thing the essential distinction between the present and that at which it is present, so that a thing's knowledge of itself does not occur due to the absence of the presence that necessitates that distinction, not due to its absence while presence—which is the reality of knowledge on the assumption—is realized. And it is answered that the annexation (al-iḍāfa) in the commentator's saying 'the reality of knowledge is what you mentioned' is like the annexation in our saying 'the reality of 'Indian' is human', not like it in our saying 'the reality of human is rational animal'. And then knowledge is a portion of the reality of presence conditioned by essential distinction, so the existence of presence does not necessitate the existence of knowledge, just as the existence of human does not necessitate the existence of 'Indian' even if its reality is that.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage contains an objection to the second approach and the subsequent discussion. The objector first challenges the definition of cognition (تعقل) as presence of quiddity, arguing it is not proven. He then concedes the definition but questions whether cognition requires a distinction (تغاير) between the knower and the known, so that a thing cannot know itself. The commentator responds by explaining that presence is a relation; if cognition were a relation, it could vary. The discussion deepens: the objector argues that mere absence of a thing from itself is not knowledge, and that distinction might be essential. The respondent clarifies that the condition of distinction is not after presence but is part of the definition of knowledge as a specific type of presence. The analogy of 'Indian' and 'human' illustrates that knowledge is a subset of presence—just as 'Indian' is a specific type of human, knowledge is a specific type of presence conditioned by essential distinction. Therefore, presence of self to self does not automatically constitute knowledge.

Key Terms
تعقل
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition alone proves the reality of a concept.
Correction: This passage shows that definition is not proof; a separate demonstration is needed.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main challenge in the first part?
A) He denies the definition of cognition.
B) He asks for proof that the reality of knowledge is presence.
C) He claims that presence is not a relation.
D) He asserts that self-knowledge is possible.

Key Takeaway
Definitions are not self-certifying; they require external proof to establish reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often accept definitions as facts—whether of knowledge, faith, or yourself. But this lesson demands proof. What belief about your own mind or soul have you never really proven? Will you let that definition remain unexamined?
?
The objector suggests self-knowledge might be impossible without distinction. When you reflect on your own self-awareness, do you sense a separation between the knower and the known, or are they one? If they are one, does that make your self-knowledge valid? Or is it a delusion?
?
The passage forces a choice: either you accept an unproven definition or you seek a proof. Right now, are you living by definitions of God, the afterlife, or your own purpose that you have never seriously challenged? What would it cost you to demand proof for them—and what would it cost not to?

Presence as Relation and the Condition of Distinction
Discussion on presence as relation and the necessary condition of distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether presence is a relation and how the condition of distinction affects self-knowledge.

Core Idea
If presence is a relation, cognition could be a special type of relation requiring distinction.

The objector’s initial challenge leads to a deeper philosophical discussion: is presence a relation? If so, cognition could be a relational state that varies. The commentator also considers the intuitive understanding of knowledge and why mere absence of absence is not enough. This lesson unpacks these points.

Guided Path
1
Examine the objection to presence as relation
Read the commentary where someone asks: 'How (can that be) while presence is a relation?'
Think about why the philosophers might not consider cognition a relative category.
2
Understand the alternative possibility
If cognition were a relation, it could be a different relational state that occurs in us but not in abstract beings.
This opens the door for variation in cognition.
3
Analyze the support for distinction
The objector claims that everyone necessarily understands knowledge, and mere absence of a thing from itself is not knowledge.
This uses common sense to argue that self-presence is not automatically knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد على المسلك الثاني لا نسلم أن التعقل ما ذكرتم وتعريفه بذلك لا يوجب الجزم بأن حقيقته ذلك ما لم يقم عليه برهان إذ غايته أنهم يعنون بالتعقل ذلك المعنى الذي عرفوه به ولكن من أين لهم أن الحالة التي نجدها أنفسنا ونسميها العلم حقيقته ذلك الذي ذكروه لا بد له من دليل سلمناه أي سلمنا أن حقيقة العلم ما ذكرتموه لكن لم لا يجوز يشترط فيه التغاير بين الحاضر وما حضر هو عنده فلا يكون الشيء عالما بنفسه

قوله : (لا نسلم أن التعقل ما ذكرتم إلخ ) قيل : كيف والحضور نسبة بين الشيئين ولم يقل أحد من أن الفلاسفة بكون التعقل والعلم من المقولات النسبية، ولو جوز كون التعقل نسبة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون عبارة عن حالة نسبة أخرى يتحصل في حقنا دون بعض المجردات ؟

قوله : ( لكن لم لا يجوز أن يشترط فيه التغاير) قد يؤيد هذا بأن العلم مما يفهم بالضرورة كل أحد إما بكنهه أو بما يميز عن سائر أغياره، ونحن نعلم قطعا أن مجرد عدم غيبية الشيء عن نفسه الذي سموه بالحضور عند نفسه سواء كان مجرداً أو مادياً ليس مما يصدق عليه هذا المفهوم بل ربما يدعي أن عدم غيبية الشيء عن نفسه ليس فيه تفاوت بين المجرد وغيره، بحيث يكون أحدهما علماً والآخر غير علم قيل : المفهوم من تقرير الشارح أنه حمل هذا الكلام على أن التغاير شرط بعد تحقق الحضور ولا وجه له لأنه يؤدي إلى أن يوجد حقيقة الشيء بدونه لانتفاء شرطه وهو غير معقول. فالصواب أن مراد المصنف لم لا يجوز أن يشترط في حضور الشيء للشيء المغايرة الذاتية بين الحاضر وما حضر عنده حتى لا يتعلق علم الشيء بنفسه لانتفاء الحضور المستلزم لتلك المغايرة لا لانتفائها مع تحقق الحضور الذي هو حقيقة العلم على الفرض وأجيب بأن الإضافة في قول الشارح حقيقة العلم ما ذكرتموه مثل الإضافة في قولنا : حقيقة الهندي الإنسان لا مثلها في قولنا : حقيقة الإنسان الحيوان الناطق، وحينئذ يكون العلم حصة من حقيقة الحضور مشروطاً بالمغايرة الذاتية فلا يلزم من وجود الحضور وجود العلم كما لا يلزم من وجود الإنسان وجود الهندي وإن كان حقيقته ذلك .
And an objection is made against the second approach: We do not concede that cognition is what you mentioned, and defining it thus does not necessitate certainty that its reality is that, unless a proof is established for it; for the utmost is that they mean by cognition that meaning which they defined, but whence have they that the state which we find in ourselves and call knowledge its reality is that which they mentioned? It needs a proof. We concede it, i.e., we concede that the reality of knowledge is what you mentioned, but why may it not be that distinction between the present and that at which it is present is required in it, so that a thing is not knowing of itself?

His saying: (We do not concede that cognition is what you mentioned, etc.) It is said: How (can that be) while presence is a relation between two things, and none of the philosophers say that cognition and knowledge are from the relative categories? And if it is allowed that cognition is a relation, then why may it not be an expression of another relational state that occurs in us but not in some abstract beings?

His saying: (But why may it not be that distinction is required in it?) This may be supported by the fact that knowledge is understood necessarily by everyone, either through its essence or through what distinguishes it from other things; and we know with certainty that mere absence of a thing's being absent from itself, which they call presence to itself, whether it be abstract or material, is not something to which this concept applies; rather, it might be claimed that the absence of a thing's being absent from itself has no difference between abstract and other, such that one is knowledge and the other is not knowledge. It is said: What is understood from the commentator's exposition is that he carried this speech on the view that distinction is a condition after the realization of presence, and there is no sense to it because it leads to the existence of the reality of the thing without it due to the negation of its condition, and that is unintelligible. So the correct view is that the author meant: why may it not be required in the presence of a thing to a thing the essential distinction between the present and that at which it is present, so that a thing's knowledge of itself does not occur due to the absence of the presence that necessitates that distinction, not due to its absence while presence—which is the reality of knowledge on the assumption—is realized. And it is answered that the annexation (al-iḍāfa) in the commentator's saying 'the reality of knowledge is what you mentioned' is like the annexation in our saying 'the reality of 'Indian' is human', not like it in our saying 'the reality of human is rational animal'. And then knowledge is a portion of the reality of presence conditioned by essential distinction, so the existence of presence does not necessitate the existence of knowledge, just as the existence of human does not necessitate the existence of 'Indian' even if its reality is that.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage contains an objection to the second approach and the subsequent discussion. The objector first challenges the definition of cognition (تعقل) as presence of quiddity, arguing it is not proven. He then concedes the definition but questions whether cognition requires a distinction (تغاير) between the knower and the known, so that a thing cannot know itself. The commentator responds by explaining that presence is a relation; if cognition were a relation, it could vary. The discussion deepens: the objector argues that mere absence of a thing from itself is not knowledge, and that distinction might be essential. The respondent clarifies that the condition of distinction is not after presence but is part of the definition of knowledge as a specific type of presence. The analogy of 'Indian' and 'human' illustrates that knowledge is a subset of presence—just as 'Indian' is a specific type of human, knowledge is a specific type of presence conditioned by essential distinction. Therefore, presence of self to self does not automatically constitute knowledge.

Key Terms
حضور
نسبة
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a thing is present to itself, it automatically knows itself.
Correction: The passage argues that mere presence is not sufficient; distinction is required for knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why do philosophers object to considering cognition a relative category?
A) Because presence is not a relation.
B) Because they do not consider knowledge a relation.
C) Because cognition is a substance.
D) Because relative categories are not real.

Key Takeaway
The debate explores whether presence is a relation and whether self-presence automatically constitutes knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly know yourself, or are you merely present to yourself? If self-presence is not knowledge, then what have you actually learned about your own soul? How will you seek real self-knowledge that goes beyond mere awareness?
?
If the philosophers are right that knowledge requires a distinction between knower and known, then what does it mean for you to claim that you 'know' God? Have you mistaken a feeling of presence for true knowledge, and what will you do to seek real distinction-based knowledge of your Lord?

Resolution: Knowledge as a Specific Type of Presence
Final resolution using the Indian/human analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final resolution that knowledge is a portion of presence conditioned by essential distinction, using the analogy of 'Indian' and 'human'.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a specific type of presence, not just any presence.

After the objections, the commentator provides a clarification. He explains that the condition of distinction is not added after presence but is part of the definition of knowledge as a specific kind of presence. The analogy of 'Indian' and 'human' illustrates this: knowledge is to presence as Indian is to human—a subset with a defining condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the correction of interpretation
Read the part where the commentator says the correct view is that distinction is required for presence itself, not as an added condition.
Notice the distinction between presence simpliciter and presence qualified by distinction.
2
Understand the analogy
Knowledge is like 'Indian' which is a portion of 'human' but not every human is Indian.
Similarly, presence exists in many forms, but only some are knowledge.
3
Apply the resolution
See how this resolves the objection: self-presence exists but does not meet the condition of essential distinction, so it is not knowledge.
This preserves the possibility that a thing can be present to itself without knowing itself.

Objection on Knowledge of Cause and Effect
Objection and reply on causality and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection to the claim that knowledge of cause entails knowledge of effect, and the nuanced replies involving intentional knowledge and complete knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a cause does not automatically give knowledge of all its effects.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the second approach discussed earlier. The objector argues that if knowing the cause entailed knowing the effect, then knowing one thing would require knowing all its necessary concomitants, which is refuted by experience. The reply distinguishes between different senses of 'necessary concomitant' and introduces the condition of intentional conception. Follow along as we unpack this layered argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its consequence
Read the initial objection: 'We concede that distinction is not required, but we do not concede that knowledge of cause necessitates knowledge of effect; otherwise it would lead to knowledge of all necessary concomitants.'
Notice the immediate absurd consequence.
2
Examine the reply on 'necessary concomitant'
The reply clarifies that 'lāzim' here means dependent or effect in a general sense, not all concomitants.
This narrows the definition.
3
Trace the objection about intentional knowledge
The further objection: even if we accept the reply, knowledge of the effect requires intentional conception; otherwise it does not follow.
Intentional knowledge is key.
4
Understand the conclusion
The proof may establish essential knowledge but not intentional knowledge of effects, unless complete knowledge of cause includes causality and existence.
The argument ends with a nuanced position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمناه أي عدم اشتراط التغاير لكن لا نسلم أن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول وإلا لزم من العلم بالشيء العلم بجميع لوازمه القريبة والبعيدة لأنه إذا علم الشيء علم لازمه القريب الذي هو معلوله وإذا علما معاً علم البعيد أيضاً لأنه معلولهما . نعم يلزم ذلك إذا علم الشيء الذي هو علة وعلم أنه علة له أي الآخر الذي هو معلول وعلم أنه موجود و علم أنه يلزم من وجود العلة وجود المعلول

قوله : ( بجميع لوازمه القريبة والبعيدة نوقش فيه بأن جميع لوازم الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون معلولات له بل قد يكون معلولاً وقد يكون علة وقد يكون غيرهما وأجيب بأن اللازم قد يطلق على ما يتبع الشيء ويحتاج إليه وهو بهذا المعني معلول لذلك الشيء في الجملة وهو المراد هاهنا . قوله : ( وإذا علم ما علم البعيد أيضاً فإن قلت : العلم باللازم إنما يلزم إذا تصور الملزوم ويكون العلم به تاماً بالمعنى الذي يعرفه وحينئذ نقول : العلم الثالث إنما يلزم إذا كان تصور اللازم الأول قصدياً وليس فليس وإن تصور كذلك نقول : يلزم الثالث لهما وهلم جراً، لكن ينقطع بانقطاع التصورات القصدية . قلت : فحينئذ نقول : الدليل الذي ذكر لو سلم دلالته على علمه القصدي بذاته تعالى لم يدل على علمه القصدي بمعلولاته فلا يثبت المطلوب. قوله : ( وعلم أنه موجود وعلم أنه يلزم إلخ) فيه أن المطلوب من الدليل السابق كون المقصود هاهنا إثبات علمه تعالى بنفس معلولاته إلا بوجودها فالعلمان الأخيران مما لا حاجة إليه . اللهم إلا أن يقال : لما كان مبنى كلامهم أن العلم التام بالعلة يستلزم العلم بالمعلول، والعلم التام بها عبارة عن العلم بها بجميع ما لها ومن جملته عليتها ووجودها كان لزوم العلم بوجود المعلول داخلا في المدعى .
We concede it, i.e., the non-requirement of distinction, but we do not concede that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect; otherwise it would follow from knowledge of a thing knowledge of all its near and far necessary concomitants, because when one knows a thing, one knows its near necessary concomitant which is its effect, and when both are known, the far is also known because it is the effect of both. Yes, that follows when one knows the thing which is a cause, and knows that it is a cause of it, i.e., the other which is the effect, and knows that it exists, and knows that from the existence of the cause follows the existence of the effect.

His saying: (with all its near and far necessary concomitants) It is objected to this that not all necessary concomitants of a thing are necessarily its effects; rather, it may be an effect, or a cause, or other than both. And it is answered that 'necessary concomitant' (lāzim) may be applied to what follows a thing and is dependent on it, and in this sense it is an effect of that thing in general, and that is what is meant here.

His saying: (and when he knows, he knows the far also) For if you say: knowledge of the necessary concomitant only follows if one has a conception of the substrate (malzūm) and the knowledge of it is complete in the sense which they know; then we say: the third knowledge only follows if the conception of the first necessary concomitant is intentional (qaṣdī), and if not, then it does not; and if it is conceived thus, we say: the third follows from both, and so on, but it stops with the cessation of intentional conceptions. I say: then we say: the proof that was mentioned, even if we concede its indication of His intentional knowledge of His own essence, exalted be He, does not indicate His intentional knowledge of His effects, so the desired conclusion is not established.

His saying: (and that he knows it exists and that he knows it follows, etc.) In it is that the object of the previous proof is that the intended here is to prove His knowledge, exalted be He, of the very essences of His effects, not their existence, so the two latter pieces of knowledge are not needed. Unless it is said: since their speech is based on the fact that complete knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, and complete knowledge of it is knowledge of it with all that belongs to it, including its causality and existence, then the necessity of knowledge of the existence of the effect is included in the claim.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the second approach's claim that knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect. The objector argues that if that were true, then from knowledge of one thing we would know all its necessary concomitants (لوازم), both near and far, which is absurd. The commentator replies that 'necessary concomitant' here means that which depends on the thing, so it is an effect in a general sense. A further objection arises: even if we concede that, the knowledge of the effect would require intentional (قصدي) conception; otherwise, it does not follow. The discussion concludes that the proof might establish God's essential knowledge but not His intentional knowledge of effects, unless complete knowledge of the cause includes its causality and existence, thereby including knowledge of the effect's existence.

Key Terms
لازم
معلول
علة
قصدية
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowledge of a cause automatically yields knowledge of all its effects without any conditions.
Correction: The passage shows that it requires conditions such as intentional conception and complete knowledge of the cause including its causality and existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection regarding knowledge of the cause entailing knowledge of the effect?
A) It would lead to knowledge of all necessary concomitants of the thing.
B) The cause is unknown.
C) The effect is not related to the cause.
D) Knowledge of the cause is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a cause does not entail knowledge of all its effects without additional conditions such as intentional conception and complete knowledge of the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans, do you often assume that because you know the cause (your effort, resources, etc.), you know the outcome? How does this lesson's distinction between knowledge of cause and knowledge of effect challenge that assumption? What does it demand you change about how you rely on God's will in your daily planning?
?
This lesson distinguishes between intentional and non-intentional knowledge. ?

Intentional Conception Required for Knowledge of Necessary Consequences
Objection and answer about the necessity of intentional conception for knowledge of remote entailments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of a cause alone does not yield knowledge of all remote consequences without intentional acts of conception.

Core Idea
Knowledge of consequences requires intentional conception at each step.

The author here addresses an important objection to the argument for God's knowledge. If God knows His own essence, does He automatically know all effects? The objection points out that knowledge of remote consequences depends on intentionally conceiving intermediate steps. This passage clarifies the need for intentional conception in chains of necessary entailments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage once to understand the structure: an objection followed by an answer. The objection posits a chain of knowledge from a cause to remote consequences.
Notice the conditional phrasing: 'only if' and 'if and only if'.
2
Identify the key condition
The objection states that knowledge of a necessary consequence (lazim) only follows if the thing it depends on (malzum) is conceived, and that conception must be intentional (qasdi).
This condition applies at each step of the chain.
3
Trace the chain's termination
The chain of intentional conceptions can terminate, breaking the entailment. The objection asserts that without continuous intentional conception, remote consequences are not known.
4
Understand the answer
The answer concedes this logic: even if the argument shows God's intentional knowledge of His essence, it does not prove intentional knowledge of all effects, since the chain would require intentional conceptions that may not occur.
Thus the desired conclusion is not established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإذا علم ما علم البعيد أيضاً فإن قلت : العلم باللازم إنما يلزم إذا تصور الملزوم ويكون العلم به تاماً بالمعنى الذي يعرفه وحينئذ نقول : العلم الثالث إنما يلزم إذا كان تصور اللازم الأول قصدياً وليس فليس وإن تصور كذلك نقول : يلزم الثالث لهما وهلم جراً، لكن ينقطع بانقطاع التصورات القصدية . قلت : فحينئذ نقول : الدليل الذي ذكر لو سلم دلالته على علمه القصدي بذاته تعالى لم يدل على علمه القصدي بمعلولاته فلا يثبت المطلوب.
His saying: (And when he knows what is also remote...) Then you say: Knowledge of the necessary consequence only follows if the thing of which it is a consequence is conceived, and knowledge of it is complete in the sense that he knows. In that case we say: The third knowledge only follows if the conception of the first necessary consequence is intentional ('qasdi'), and if it is not, then it is not. And if it is conceived intentionally, we say: The third follows from both, and so on, but it terminates with the termination of intentional conceptions. Then I say: In that case we say: The argument mentioned, even if it proves His intentional knowledge of His own essence, does not prove His intentional knowledge of His effects, so the desired conclusion is not established.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer about the chain of entailments when knowing a cause. The objection states that knowledge of a remote necessary consequence (e.g., the effect of an effect) only follows if the intermediate (the first consequence) is intentionally (qasdi) conceived. If one does not actively conceive the intermediate, the chain breaks. The answer concedes this point: even if the argument shows that God has intentional knowledge of His own essence (the cause), it does not prove that He has intentional knowledge of all His effects, because that would require a chain of intentional conceptions that may not occur. Thus, the argument fails to establish the desired conclusion of God's intentional knowledge of all things.

Key Terms
القصدي
التصور
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of a cause automatically yields knowledge of all remote consequences without any act of conception.
Correction: Knowledge of a remote necessary consequence requires intentional conception of each intermediate step; without it, the entailment chain breaks.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument about knowledge of the cause fail to prove God's intentional knowledge of His effects?
A) Because God's knowledge is not intentional.
B) Because knowledge of remote consequences requires a chain of intentional conceptions that may not occur.
C) Because effects are not logically entailed by the cause.
D) Because the cause is not known completely.

Key Takeaway
The argument for God's intentional knowledge of all things fails because it requires a completed chain of intentional conceptions that may not obtain.

Why Knowledge of the Cause's Existence and Causality Matters
Objection and answer on necessity of knowing cause's existence and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why perfect knowledge of a cause includes knowledge of its existence and causality, and how that relates to proving God's knowledge of effects.

Core Idea
Perfect knowledge of a cause includes its existence and causality, making knowledge of effects necessary.

This passage addresses a potential objection to a previous argument about divine knowledge. The objection claims that proving God's knowledge of effects does not require additional knowledge of the cause's existence and causality. The response clarifies that perfect knowledge of the cause inherently includes those aspects, thus validating the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify why the objector thinks the two additional knowledges (existence and entailment) are unnecessary.
Notice that the objection is about the scope of the previous argument.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'perfect knowledge' (العلم التام) and what it entails according to the answer.
Perfect knowledge is defined as knowledge of the cause with all its aspects.
3
Trace the answer's logic
See how the answer uses the definition of perfect knowledge to include causality and existence, thereby making knowledge of the effect's existence part of the claim.
This resolves the objection by showing the additional knowledges are not extra but inherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعلم أنه موجود وعلم أنه يلزم إلخ) فيه أن المطلوب من الدليل السابق كون المقصود هاهنا إثبات علمه تعالى بنفس معلولاته إلا بوجودها فالعلمان الأخيران مما لا حاجة إليه . اللهم إلا أن يقال : لما كان مبنى كلامهم أن العلم التام بالعلة يستلزم العلم بالمعلول، والعلم التام بها عبارة عن العلم بها بجميع ما لها ومن جملته عليتها ووجودها كان لزوم العلم بوجود المعلول داخلا في المدعى .
His saying: (And knowledge that it exists and knowledge that it entails, etc.) In it [there is the point] that what is sought from the previous argument is that the intended here is to prove His knowledge, exalted, of His effects themselves, not their existence, so the two latter knowledges are unnecessary. But it may be said: Since the basis of their discourse is that perfect knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, and perfect knowledge of it is knowledge of it with all that belongs to it, including its causality and existence, then the necessity of knowledge of the existence of the effect is included in the claim.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning whether knowledge of the cause's existence and its causal relation (i.e., 'it entails') are required. The objection argues that the desired conclusion is only that God knows His effects themselves, not their existence, so requiring two additional knowledges (existence and entailment) is unnecessary. The answer counters that perfect knowledge of a cause includes knowledge of all its aspects, including its causality (being a cause) and its existence. Therefore, knowledge of the effect's existence follows from that perfect knowledge, and this is part of the claim. Thus, the two additional knowledges are actually part of the perfect knowledge.

Key Terms
العلية
الوجود
العلم التام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of the effect's existence is an additional piece of information beyond perfect knowledge of the cause.
Correction: Perfect knowledge of the cause includes its existence and causality, so knowledge of the effect's existence is entailed by that perfect knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does 'perfect knowledge' of the cause include according to the answer?
A) Knowledge of the cause's essence only.
B) Knowledge of the cause's existence and its causality.
C) Knowledge of the effect's essence without its existence.
D) Knowledge of the cause's attributes except causality.

Key Takeaway
Perfect knowledge of a cause inherently includes its existence and causality, making knowledge of the effect's existence a necessary consequence.

The Objection from Self-Knowledge Only
Initial objection and refutation of al-Maqāṣid's answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial objection that the argument only proves God's self-knowledge, not knowledge of effects, and the refutation based on the concept of perfect knowledge.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge alone does not prove knowledge of effects unless self-knowledge is perfect.

In this passage, the author raises a critical objection: the arguments so far only show that God knows His own essence, not that He knows anything else. He then considers a response from al-Maqāṣid that God's self-knowledge is perfect and includes all attributes, but argues that this has not been proven.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objection: 'But what you mentioned indicates that He knows His essence... and does not indicate the establishment of the other knowledge.'
Notice the objector claims the proof only covers self-knowledge.
2
Understand the concept of perfect knowledge
Identify the definition of perfect knowledge given by the author of al-Maqāṣid: knowledge of a thing with all its attributes, including causality.
Perfect knowledge of the cause would entail knowledge of the effect.
3
See the refutation
The author's refutation: the proof does not establish that God's self-knowledge is perfect in that sense, even if it is perfect in reality.
The proof's conclusion is limited; it does not prove the perfection claimed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن ما ذكرتم يدل على أنه عالم ذاته الحاضرة عنده ولا يدل على ثبوت العلم الآخر . ( فلم قلتم : بذات العلة التي هي إن ذلك ) كله ( حاصل (له حتى يتم مطلوبكم ؟ ) وأما وجه قوله: (لكن ما ذكرتم دفع لجواب صاحب المقاصد بأن الكلام في العلم التام ما له يعني العلم بالشيء بجميع أي من الصفات التي منها العلية، ولا شك أن علم الباري تعالى بذاته كذلك وإنه يستلزم العلم بالمعلول، ووجه الدفع الذي أشار إليه أنه لا يلزم من الدليل المذكور كون علمه تعالى بذاته تاماً بذلك المعنى . وإن كان كذلك في نفس الأمر. فإن قلت : لما كانت العلة لذاته المخصوصة موجبة للمعلول المخصوص كان العلم بحقيقتها موجباً للعلم بالمعلول، وهذا ضروري لمنعه . قلت : المعلوم لنا هو أن عين العلة الخارجية مستلزمة لعين المعلول الخارجي وأما أن صورتها مستلزمة لصورته فلا نسلم ذلك إذ الأعيان تخالف الصور في كثير من الأحكام على أن علمه تعالى بذاته حضوري عندهم. فلا حصول صورة فيه. فإن قلت لما ثبت أن المعنى بكون الماهية معقولة حضورها للذات المجرد القائم بذاته تعالى لزم كون عليته معقولة لذاته ضرورة حضورها له تعالى لكونها وصفاً له . ثم إنه يلزم من علمه بها علمه بالمعلولات وهو المطلوب . قلت : أجيب عنه بأنا لا نسلم أن العلية حاضرة له تعالى لأن حضور الشيء إما بوجوده له متأصلا كصفاته الحقيقية الخارجية أو غير متأصل كما إذا الأشياء الخارجية فيه والعلية وصف اعتباري ليس لها وجود خارجي فليس لها حضور باعتبار وجودها له تعالى متأصلاً، ثم إن اتصاف الموصوف بصفة لا تقتضي ثبوت الصفة فيه ظلياً أيضاً، فلم يلزم كون العلية معقولة له أصلاً. وهذا الجواب غير مرض عندي إذ لو اعتبر في حضور الاعتباريات وجودها الظلي لم يبق فرق بين الحصولي والحضوري في علمه تعالى بالمعدومات وعاد ما هربوا عنه من لزوم التكثر في ذاته تعالى وإن التزمه أبو علي وراغم أصول الفلاسفة كما فصلناه في موقف الأعراض فالجواب عن السؤال عندي : منع أن العلم الحضوري بالعلية يستلزم العلم بالمعلول، وأن استلزام العلم الحصولي بها علمه وهذا الاستلزام هو مبنى الاستدلال، وعلمها الحصولي هو الممنوع في الجواب على أن حضور عليته له تعالى لا يستلزم حضور علية معلوله لما بعده له فلا يلزم عموم علمه تعالى مع أنهم يستدلون بهذا الدليل عليه كما لا يخفى .
But what you mentioned indicates that He knows His essence which is present to Him, and does not indicate the establishment of the other knowledge. (So why did you say: by the essence of the cause that is...) all of that is (present?) to Him so that your claim is complete?) And as for the aspect of his saying: (But what you mentioned is a refutation of the answer of the author of al-Maqāṣid that the discussion is about perfect knowledge which is knowledge of the thing with all the attributes, including causality, and there is no doubt that the knowledge of the Exalted Creator of His essence is like that and that it entails knowledge of the effect. The aspect of the refutation that he pointed to is that it does not follow from the mentioned proof that His knowledge of His essence is perfect in that sense, even if it is so in reality. For if you say: Since the cause by its specific essence necessitates the specific effect, knowledge of its reality necessitates knowledge of the effect, and this is necessary to deny. I say: What is known to us is that the external entity of the cause entails the external entity of the effect, but as for its form entailing its form, we do not grant that because external things differ from forms in many rulings, especially since His knowledge of His essence is presential (hudūrī) according to them, so there is no form in it. For if you say: Since it is established that the meaning of the quiddity being intelligible is its presence to the abstract self-subsistent essence of the Exalted, then necessarily its causality is intelligible to its essence due to its presence to Him as an attribute of Him. Then knowledge of it entails knowledge of the effects, which is the desired. I say: It was answered that we do not grant that causality is present to Him because presence of a thing is either by its existence in Him genuinely, like His real external attributes, or in a shadowy way, as external things [exist] in Him. And causality is a mental consideration (i‘tibārī) with no external existence, so it has no presence by way of existence in Him genuinely. Moreover, the qualification of a qualified object by an attribute does not necessitate the attribute having shadowy existence in it either, so causality is not intelligible to Him at all. This answer is not satisfactory to me, for if shadowy existence is considered in the presence of mental considerations, there would be no difference between conceptual (husūlī) and presential (hudūrī) knowledge in His knowledge of non-existent things, and we would return to what they fled from of necessitating multiplicity in His essence, even if Abū ‘Alī accepted it and opposed the principles of the philosophers as we detailed in the Station of Accidents. So the answer to the question according to me is: denial that presential knowledge of causality entails knowledge of the effect, and that the entailment of conceptual knowledge of it entails His knowledge, and this entailment is the basis of the inference, and conceptual knowledge of it is what is denied in the answer. Moreover, the presence of His causality to Him does not entail the presence of the causality of His effect due to what comes after, so it does not entail the universality of His knowledge, even though they use this proof for it, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a sustained objection and response concerning whether the previous arguments prove God's knowledge of His effects. The objector claims the proof only shows God's knowledge of His own essence, not knowledge of other things as distinct objects. The commentator then discusses various angles. First, a refutation of the answer of the author of al-Maqāṣid: perfect knowledge of the cause includes knowledge of all its attributes like causality, and God's self-knowledge should be perfect. But the refutation argues that the proof does not establish that God's self-knowledge is perfect in that sense. Then a sub-objection claims that knowledge of the essence of the cause necessarily gives knowledge of the effect. The answer distinguishes between the external cause (which entails the external effect) and the mental form of the cause (which does not entail the mental form of the effect), especially since God's knowledge is presential (hudūrī) without forms. Another objection argues that since the quiddity's intelligibility is its presence to God, the attribute of causality is also present. The answer denies that causality is present because it is a mental consideration (i‘tibārī) and has no external or shadowy existence. However, the author (the commentator) finds this answer unsatisfactory because it would collapse the distinction between conceptual and presential knowledge regarding non-existent things. The author's own preferred answer is: presential knowledge of causality does not entail knowledge of the effect, and conceptual knowledge of causality (which is the basis of the inference) is not proven. Therefore, the argument fails to establish universal knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم التام
العلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God knows His essence, He automatically knows everything else.
Correction: The passage argues that this automatic entailment only follows if self-knowledge is perfect in a specific sense, which the proof does not establish.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the proof not show that God's self-knowledge is perfect?
A) Because God's essence is unknowable.
B) Because the proof only shows knowledge of the essence, not that it includes all attributes.
C) Because perfect knowledge is impossible for God.
D) Because effects are not real.

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the inference from self-knowledge to knowledge of effects, and the attempted refutation via perfect knowledge is not accepted as proven.

?

?

The Causality Entailment Objection and Response
Sub-objection and answer about causal entailment in knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that knowledge of the cause's essence necessarily gives knowledge of the effect, and the response distinguishing external cause from mental form.

Core Idea
The external cause entails the effect, but its mental form does not.

A further objection is raised: since the cause necessitates its effect, knowledge of the cause should give knowledge of the effect. The response distinguishes between the external cause (which entails the effect externally) and the mental form of the cause (which does not entail the form of the effect). This distinction is crucial for understanding God's presential knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: 'If the cause by its specific essence necessitates the specific effect, then knowledge of its reality necessitates knowledge of the effect.'
The objector assumes a straightforward entailment.
2
Understand the response
The response: the external cause entails the external effect, but the form of the cause does not entail the form of the effect.
This is because external things differ from forms in many rulings.
3
Connect to presential knowledge
Note that God's knowledge is presential (hudūrī), meaning no mental forms exist in Him. So the form-based entailment does not apply.
Hence, the objection fails.

But what you mentioned indicates that He knows His essence which is present to Him, and does not indicate the establishment of the other knowledge. (So why did you say: by the essence of the cause that is...) all of that is (present?) to Him so that your claim is complete?) And as for the aspect of his saying: (But what you mentioned is a refutation of the answer of the author of al-Maqāṣid that the discussion is about perfect knowledge which is knowledge of the thing with all the attributes, including causality, and there is no doubt that the knowledge of the Exalted Creator of His essence is like that and that it entails knowledge of the effect. The aspect of the refutation that he pointed to is that it does not follow from the mentioned proof that His knowledge of His essence is perfect in that sense, even if it is so in reality. For if you say: Since the cause by its specific essence necessitates the specific effect, knowledge of its reality necessitates knowledge of the effect, and this is necessary to deny. I say: What is known to us is that the external entity of the cause entails the external entity of the effect, but as for its form entailing its form, we do not grant that because external things differ from forms in many rulings, especially since His knowledge of His essence is presential (hudūrī) according to them, so there is no form in it. For if you say: Since it is established that the meaning of the quiddity being intelligible is its presence to the abstract self-subsistent essence of the Exalted, then necessarily its causality is intelligible to its essence due to its presence to Him as an attribute of Him. Then knowledge of it entails knowledge of the effects, which is the desired. I say: It was answered that we do not grant that causality is present to Him because presence of a thing is either by its existence in Him genuinely, like His real external attributes, or in a shadowy way, as external things [exist] in Him. And causality is a mental consideration (i‘tibārī) with no external existence, so it has no presence by way of existence in Him genuinely. Moreover, the qualification of a qualified object by an attribute does not necessitate the attribute having shadowy existence in it either, so causality is not intelligible to Him at all. This answer is not satisfactory to me, for if shadowy existence is considered in the presence of mental considerations, there would be no difference between conceptual (husūlī) and presential (hudūrī) knowledge in His knowledge of non-existent things, and we would return to what they fled from of necessitating multiplicity in His essence, even if Abū ‘Alī accepted it and opposed the principles of the philosophers as we detailed in the Station of Accidents. So the answer to the question according to me is: denial that presential knowledge of causality entails knowledge of the effect, and that the entailment of conceptual knowledge of it entails His knowledge, and this entailment is the basis of the inference, and conceptual knowledge of it is what is denied in the answer. Moreover, the presence of His causality to Him does not entail the presence of the causality of His effect due to what comes after, so it does not entail the universality of His knowledge, even though they use this proof for it, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a sustained objection and response concerning whether the previous arguments prove God's knowledge of His effects. The objector claims the proof only shows God's knowledge of His own essence, not knowledge of other things as distinct objects. The commentator then discusses various angles. First, a refutation of the answer of the author of al-Maqāṣid: perfect knowledge of the cause includes knowledge of all its attributes like causality, and God's self-knowledge should be perfect. But the refutation argues that the proof does not establish that God's self-knowledge is perfect in that sense. Then a sub-objection claims that knowledge of the essence of the cause necessarily gives knowledge of the effect. The answer distinguishes between the external cause (which entails the external effect) and the mental form of the cause (which does not entail the mental form of the effect), especially since God's knowledge is presential (hudūrī) without forms. Another objection argues that since the quiddity's intelligibility is its presence to God, the attribute of causality is also present. The answer denies that causality is present because it is a mental consideration (i‘tibārī) and has no external or shadowy existence. However, the author (the commentator) finds this answer unsatisfactory because it would collapse the distinction between conceptual and presential knowledge regarding non-existent things. The author's own preferred answer is: presential knowledge of causality does not entail knowledge of the effect, and conceptual knowledge of causality (which is the basis of the inference) is not proven. Therefore, the argument fails to establish universal knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم الحضوري
الصورة العلمية
العلية

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does knowledge of the cause's form not give knowledge of the effect's form?
A) Because forms are identical to external objects.
B) Because external things differ from forms in many rulings.
C) Because God doesn't know causes.
D) Because effects don't have forms.

Key Takeaway
The entailment between cause and effect holds externally but not for their forms, especially in the context of God's presential knowledge.

?

?
If God's knowledge is presential and without form, what does that imply about your own attempts to comprehend His essence through concepts? Will you humble yourself and acknowledge the limits of your mental representations?
?
The objector assumed that mental entailment mirrors external entailment. In what area of your life are you confusing your mental models with reality? Will you actively seek to align your thoughts with what is actual, not just what seems logical to you?

The Author's Final Answer on Presential Knowledge and Causality
Final objection, two answers, critique, and author's solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that causality must be present to God as an attribute, the initial response that causality is a mental consideration (i‘tibārī) and not present, the critique of that response, and the author's own solution.

Core Idea
Presential knowledge of causality does not entail knowledge of effects; only conceptual knowledge would.

The discussion intensifies as another objection argues that since quiddity is present to God, its attribute of causality must also be present. Two answers are explored: one denying causality's presence because it is a mental consideration, and another—the author's own—denying that presential knowledge of causality entails knowledge of effects in a way that proves universality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection: if the quiddity is intelligible through its presence to God, then its attribute of causality is also present, leading to knowledge of effects.
This assumes causality is a present attribute.
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer denies that causality is present because it is a mental consideration (i‘tibārī) without external existence, and thus not subject to presential presence.
This relies on the distinction between genuine attributes and mental considerations.
3
See the author's critique
The author finds this answer unsatisfactory because if mental considerations could have shadowy presence, the distinction between conceptual and presential knowledge collapses for non-existents.
This would lead to multiplicity in God's essence, which is problematic.
4
Learn the author's own answer
The author's answer: presential knowledge of causality does not entail knowledge of the effect; only conceptual knowledge would, but that is not granted.
Thus, the argument for universal knowledge fails.

The Theologians' Method: God Knows Particulars and Universals Alike
Sanusi notes that the theologians' method covers both particulars and universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologians' method proves that God knows particulars and universals in the same way, based on their being effects of His will and power.

Core Idea
God knows all things because all things are His effects—both particulars and universals alike.

This lesson examines a brief but crucial note from the theologians (mutakallimūn) clarifying their position on divine knowledge. Their method establishes that God's knowledge encompasses both particulars and universals without distinction, because both are equally created by Him with perfection and are under His power. The note eliminates any misconception that the theologians' argument only covers universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording come through.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic text; they are part of the commentary's layout.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that the path of the theologians indicates knowledge of particulars just as it indicates knowledge of universals.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The reasoning is that particulars are effects (ma‘lūlāt) like universals, issuing from God with perfection and within His power. Therefore, He knows both together.
This is a concise argument; compare it to the longer treatments elsewhere in kalam.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this note is important: it shows that the theologians do not need separate proofs for divine knowledge of particulars versus universals. The same logic covers both.

Source Text

تنبيه : مسلكا المتكلمين يفيد أن العلم بالجزئيات ( كما يفيد أن العلم بالكليات وذلك ) لأن الجزئيات ) معلولة كالكليات (صادرة عنه على صفة الإتقان ومقدورة له فيكون عالماً بهما معاً (
Note: The path of the theologians (mutakallimūn) indicates that knowledge of particulars ( just as it indicates knowledge of universals, and that is because particulars are effects like universals, issuing from Him with perfection and are within His power, so He is knowing of both together.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a brief note (tanbīh) summarizing the theologians' argument for God's knowledge. The theologians hold that God knows particulars because they are His effects (ma‘lūlāt) and are within His power (maqdūrah). The note emphasizes that this same reasoning applies to universals: they are also effects issuing from Him with perfection and under His power. Therefore, the theologians' method proves that God knows both particulars and universals in a similar manner, without needing separate proofs.

The Non-Existence of Universals and Their Causality Within Individuals
A critical comment on universals' existence and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why universals cannot be directly caused by God according to Sunni theology, and how their causality reduces to the individuals they characterize.

Core Idea
Universals have no reality—neither outside the mind nor inside it—so they can only be caused through individuals.

This passage addresses a key point of contention between philosophers and theologians: the status of universals. The Sunni position denies any independent existence to universals, which has direct implications for how we understand divine causation and knowledge. Let's examine the author's argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice that the author begins by stating that the issuance of universals from God is questionable ('محل تردد').
This sets up the problem.
2
Understand the denial of existence
The author then provides the reason: universals have no existence in the external world (الخارج) nor in the mind (الذهن) according to our school.
This is a strong denial.
3
Trace the causality back to individuals
The author explains that any causation attributed to universals is actually causation of the individuals that instantiate them, and this shows the philosophers' argument actually proves divine knowledge of particulars better.
This is the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كالكليات صادرة عنه صدور الكليات عنه تعالى محل تردد إذ لا وجود لها في الخارج، ولا في الذهن عندنا . وأما كونها معللة في ضمن الأفراد فهو راجع إلى تعلل الأفراد كما أشار إليه في المقصد الثاني من بحث العلة والمعلول وبهذا يظهر أن دلالة ثاني مسلكي الحكماء على علمه تعالى بالجزئيات أظهر من دلالته على علمه تعالى بالكليات.
His saying: (like universals issuing from Him) The issuance of universals from the Exalted is a locus of hesitation since they have no existence in the external world, nor in the mind according to us. As for their being caused within individuals, that reverts to the being-caused of the individuals, as he pointed out in the Second Objective of the discussion of cause and effect. By this it appears that the indication of the second path of the philosophers (ḥukamā’) regarding His knowledge of particulars is clearer than its indication regarding His knowledge of universals.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a critical comment on the status of universals (kulliyyāt). The author notes that the very notion of universals issuing from God is questionable because, according to the Sunni theological school (the 'us'), universals have no existence in the external world (khārij) nor in the mind (dhihn). Thus, they cannot be said to be caused (ma‘lūlāt) in themselves. Rather, if they are said to be caused, it is only in the sense that they exist as aspects of individual entities, so their causality reduces to the causality of individuals. This point was already made in an earlier section. From this, the author concludes that the philosophers' second argument (which likely uses universals as a type of known object) actually proves God's knowledge of particulars more clearly than it proves His knowledge of universals, since universals lack independent reality.

Key Terms

الذهن
الأفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume universals are real entities that can be directly caused.
Correction: The passage denies their independent existence; they exist only as aspects of individuals.

Checkpoint

A) Because universals are more numerous.
B) Because universals have no independent existence, so they are only known through individuals.
C) Because the argument only mentions particulars.
D) Because philosophers reject universals entirely.

Key Takeaway
Universals have no independent existence, so their causation is only through individuals, making the philosophers' argument for divine knowledge of particulars stronger than for universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
You often think of God's knowledge as encompassing all things, but have you ever considered that He knows you not as a mere instance of a universal category, but as a unique individual with your own specific history? How does that change your relationship with Him? Commit to one change in your daily awareness of His knowledge.
