The Two Paths Yield Only Universal Knowledge and the Objection
The author explains why the two paths give only universal knowledge and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' two paths yield only universal knowledge, the contradiction in their denial of God's knowledge of particulars, and the investigator's critique of their inconsistent reasoning.

Core Idea
If complete knowledge of a cause entails knowledge of its effects, then God must know particulars—denying it is inconsistent.

This passage presents a key argument in classical Islamic philosophy about the nature of divine knowledge. The philosophers claim that their two methods—knowing a thing by its essence or by its cause—only give universal knowledge. Yet they also hold that knowing a cause completely yields knowledge of all its effects, including particulars. This creates a tension when they deny that God knows particulars. A sharp investigator exposes this contradiction and compares their maneuvering to the flawed reasoning of conjectural sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage once
Read the Arabic and translation without pausing. Get a sense of the overall argument: the claim about universal knowledge, the apparent contradiction, and the objection.
Notice the structure: a statement, a reason, then 'and here is a place for reflection' introducing the problem.
2
Understand why the paths give only universal knowledge
Focus on the first part: what is known by its essence or cause is universal because essence and being-caused are universal concepts. Tying universals together does not create a particular.
The author adds a note that the 'exclusivity' is relative—God's knowledge of Himself is particular.
3
Identify the philosophers' contradictory position
The philosophers claim complete knowledge of a cause necessitates knowledge of its effects (including particulars), yet they deny God's knowledge of particulars to avoid change in His attributes. This is the contradiction.
The investigator says they are like people of conjectural sciences who add exceptions to their rules.
4
Examine the defense and its refutation
One defense is that knowledge of the cause gives sensation, not intellectual knowledge, of corporeal particulars. The refutation: the need for physical senses applies only to us, not to God.
The passage also clarifies the background about change in attributes, referencing Abu Ali's view.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The philosophers' attempt to restrict their rational principle with a 'preventive factor' (change) is not acceptable in certain sciences. The lesson reveals an internal tension in their system.
Consider how this connects to the broader debate about divine attributes and causality.

مسلكا الحكماء فلا يوجبان إلا علماً كلياً لأن ما علم بماهيته المجردة كما استفيد من الأول أو علم بعلته كما استفيد من الثاني يعلم علماً كلياً، فإن المعلول ماهيته كذا إما وحدها كما في المسلك الأول أو مع كونها معللة بكذا كما في المسلك الثاني والماهية كلية وكونها معللة بكذا كلي أيضا وتقيد الكلي بالكلي مرات كثيرة لا يفيد الجزئية فضلا عن تفسده به مرة واحدة، وهاهنا محل تأمل فإنهم زعموا أن العلم التام بخصوصية العلة يستلزم العلم التام بخصوصيات معلولاتها الصادرة عنها بوسط أو بغير وسط وادعوا أيضاً انتفاء علمه تعالى بالجزئيات من حيث هي جزئية لاستلزامه التغير في صفاته الحقيقية . فاعترض عليهم بعض المحققين. وقال : إنهم مع ادعائهم الذكاء قد تناقض كلامهم هاهنا فإن الجزئيات معلولة له كالكليات فيلزم من قاعدتهم المذكورة علمه بها أيضاً لكنهم التجؤوا في دفعه إلى تخصيص القاعدة العقلية بسبب مانع هو التغير كما هو أرباب العلوم الظنية فإنهم يخصصون قواعدهم بموانع تمنع اطرادها وذلك مما لا يستقيم في العلوم اليقينية.
قوله : فلا يوجبان إلا علماً كلياً لعل الحصر إضافي بالنسبة إلى الجزئيات المعلولة وإلا ففي المسلك الثاني أنه تعالى يعقل ذاته ومن البين أنه علم جزئي، فتأمل .
قوله : لاستلزامه التغير في صفاته الحقيقية قد ذكرنا في مباحث العلم أن مذهب أبي علي ومتابعيه كون علم الله تعالى صفة حقيقية زائدة على ذاته تعالى وإن كان مخالفاً لقواعد الفلاسفة. فالتغير في العلم تغير في الصفة الحقيقية عندهم وجميع الصفات على هذا باعتبار خصوصيات العلم وأما غيرهم فلا ينبغي أن يحمل كلامهم على الإلزام كما نبهت فيما سبق على مثله .
قوله : قد تناقض كلامهم إلخ قد يعتذر عنه بأنهم إنما ادعوا أن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول لأن العلم بها يوجب الإحساس به وإدراك الجزئيات الجسمانية من حيث هي جزئيات جسمانية إحساس لا يمكن إلا بالحواس الجسمانية لا علم فلا تناقض ودفع هذا الاعتذار بأن كون إدراك الجزئيات الجسمانية محتاجا إلى آلات جسمانية إنما هو في حقنا لا بالنسبة إلى الواجب تعالى .
The two paths of the philosophers do not yield except universal knowledge, because what is known by its essence abstracted, as derived from the first, or known by its cause, as derived from the second, is known universally. For the effect, its essence is such, either alone as in the first path, or with being caused by such-and-such as in the second path. The essence is universal, and being caused by such-and-such is also universal, and tying the universal to the universal many times does not yield particularity, let alone corrupt it once. And here is a place for reflection: indeed they claimed that complete knowledge of the particularity of the cause necessitates complete knowledge of the particularities of its effects issuing from it with or without an intermediary, and they also claimed the negation of His knowledge, exalted be He, of particulars insofar as they are particular, because it would necessitate change in His real attributes. So a certain investigator objected to them and said: with their claim of intelligence, their statement here is contradictory, for particulars are effects of Him like universals, so from their mentioned principle knowledge of them also follows. But they resorted to specifying the rational principle due to a preventive factor, which is change, like the people of conjectural sciences: they specify their rules with preventive factors that prevent their universality, and that is not sound in certain sciences. His statement: 'so they do not yield except universal knowledge' – perhaps the exclusivity is relative with respect to the particular effects, otherwise in the second path He knows His essence, and it is evident that that is particular knowledge, so reflect. His statement: 'because it would necessitate change in His real attributes' – we have mentioned in the discussions of knowledge that the doctrine of Abu Ali and his followers is that God's knowledge is a real attribute additional to His essence, although it contradicts the principles of the philosophers. So change in knowledge is change in the real attribute according to them, and all attributes are considered based on particularities of knowledge. As for others, their words should not be interpreted as an imposition, as I warned earlier about similar. His statement: 'their statement is contradictory etc.' – it may be excused by saying that they only claimed that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect because knowledge of it necessitates sensation of it, and the perception of corporeal particulars insofar as they are corporeal particulars is sensation which is only possible through corporeal senses, not knowledge, so there is no contradiction. And this excuse is refuted by saying that the need of perceiving corporeal particulars for corporeal instruments is only with respect to us, not with respect to the Necessary Exalted.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an argument-explanation with an objection and response. It begins by stating the philosophers' claim that the two paths (the path of essence and the path of causality) yield only universal knowledge. The author explains why: what is known by its essence as abstracted from matter is universal, and what is known by its cause is also universal because the essence of the effect and its being caused are both universal concepts; tying universal to universal repeatedly does not produce particularity. Then the author points out a contradiction: the philosophers also claim that complete knowledge of the cause's particularity necessitates complete knowledge of its effects (which include particulars), yet they deny God's knowledge of particulars to avoid change in His attributes. An investigator objects that they contradict themselves—since particulars are effects of God like universals, their own principle should require knowledge of them. Their evasion is to specify the rational principle with the preventive factor of change, likening them to practitioners of probabilistic sciences who qualify their rules with exceptions, which is not acceptable in certain sciences. The commentary clarifies three points: (1) the exclusivity is relative—the second path involves God's knowledge of Himself, which is particular; (2) the background of the discussion about change in attributes refers to Abu Ali's view that knowledge is an additional real attribute; (3) a possible defense that knowledge of cause yields sensation, not intellectual knowledge, is rejected because the need for bodily instruments applies only to created beings, not to God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the philosophers' use of a preventive factor (change) to avoid the conclusion is a valid rational move, like in empirical sciences where exceptions are allowed.
Correction: The passage argues that in certain (certain) sciences, and in rational principles, such ad hoc exceptions are not acceptable. The preventive factor is not a genuine constraint but a confession of inconsistency.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction the investigator points out in the philosophers' position?
A) They claim knowledge of the cause yields only universal knowledge but admit particular knowledge of the self.
B) They claim complete knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of effects, yet deny knowledge of particulars to avoid change.
C) They claim God knows universals but not Himself.
D) They claim knowledge is a relation but then deny it can be a relation to self.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' attempt to deny God's knowledge of particulars while holding that complete cause knowledge entails effect knowledge leads to a contradiction that cannot be saved by appealing to change as a preventive factor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical inconsistency in your own beliefs—like the philosophers' contradiction here—do you tend to make rationalizing exceptions to protect your comfort, or do you honestly revise your position? What recent example comes to mind?
?
The philosophers denied God's detailed knowledge of your life to avoid change in His attributes. Have you ever limited God's knowledge or power in your heart to avoid the weight of His constant awareness of your sins? How would your life change if you fully believed He knows every particular?
?
The text condemns using 'preventive factors' to salvage weak principles. Are there areas in your faith where you excuse disobedience with 'but my situation is different'? What would it look like to drop those excuses and submit fully to the clear teachings?

The Universality of Divine Knowledge and the Objection on the Whole
A definition and proof, followed by objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians prove that God's knowledge covers all concepts and how they resolve the objection about knowledge of the whole (all existents and non-existents).

Core Idea
God's knowledge is universal; knowing the whole is knowing the parts, not a separate distinction.

This passage addresses a crucial question: if God knows all things, does He also know the 'whole' of all things as a single object? The author first establishes that God's knowledge encompasses every concept, then tackles an objection that seems to threaten the coherence of divine omniscience. The discussion reveals deep insights about the nature of knowledge and distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing; then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from assertion to proof to objection and answer.
The passage is relatively dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim and proof
Find the statement that God's knowledge encompasses all concepts. Then locate the proof: the essence of God is the cause of knowledge, and the essences of things are the cause of being known; since the relation is equal, knowing some entails knowing all.
3
Understand the objection about the whole
The objection argues that knowledge requires distinction, but there is nothing outside the whole to distinguish it from. Consider why this is a challenge to the claim that God knows the whole.
This is a logical problem: if the whole includes everything, how can it be a distinct known object?
4
Follow the proposed and correct answers
The passage dismisses the idea that knowledge of individuals alone suffices without a separate act for the whole. The correct answer is that distinction is only needed when there is an 'other'; for the whole, the other is each individual, so knowledge of individuals provides the needed distinction.
Contrast the two answers to see why one fails and the other succeeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : إن علمه تعالى يعم المفهومات كلها الممكنة والواجبة والممتنعة فهو أعم من القدرة لأنها تختص بالممكنات دون الواجبات والممتنعات وإنما قلنا : معمومه للمفهومات لمثل ما مر في القدرة وهو أن الموجب للعلم ذاته والمقتضي للمعلومية ذوات المعلومات ومفهوماتها ونسبة الذات إلى الكل سواء فإذا كان عالماً ببعضها كان عالماً بكلها.
قوله : يعم المفهومات كلها أورد على معلومية الكل أي جميع الموجودات والمعدومات لله تعالى بأن العلم نفس التميز أو صفة توجبه ولا شيء بعد الجميع يعقل تميزه عنه وما قيل: معنى للعلم بالمجموع إلا العلم بآحاده فيكفي تميز كل فرد ولا يجب تميز الكل لا يلتفت إليه لأنه إذا علم كل واحد يكون جميع الآحاد أيضاً معلوماً والمعلومية تقتضي التميز. وقد يجاب : بأن العلم يوجب تميزاً فيما يوجد هناك غير إذ لو لم يتميز المعلوم حينئذ عن الغير لم يكن هو بالمعلومية أولى وأما حيث لا غير فلا نسلم لزوم التميز، فإن التميز فرع تحقق المتميز عنه البتة، والحق في الجواب أن التميز هاهنا عن الغير الذي هو كل واحد من الأحاد.
The second research: Indeed His knowledge, exalted be He, encompasses all concepts: the possible, the necessary, and the impossible; so it is more general than power, because power is specific to possibles, not to necessary and impossible things. And we only said: its generality for concepts is like what passed in power, namely that the necessitator of knowledge is His essence, and the requisite for being known is the essences of the known things and their concepts, and the relation of the essence to all is equal; so if He is knowing of some of them, He is knowing of all of them. His statement: 'encompasses all concepts' – an objection was raised about the knowledge of the whole, i.e., all existents and non-existents for God, that knowledge is distinction itself or an attribute that necessitates it, and there is nothing after the whole whose distinction from it could be conceived. And what was said: 'the meaning of knowledge of the aggregate is nothing but knowledge of its individuals, so distinction of each individual suffices and distinction of the whole is not necessary' is not paid attention to, because if each one is known, all the individuals are also known, and being known necessitates distinction. And it may be answered: that knowledge necessitates distinction when there is otherness, for if the known were not distinguished from the other, it would not be more entitled to being known; but where there is no other, we do not admit the necessity of distinction, for distinction is a branch of the existence of that from which it is distinguished absolutely. And the correct answer is that distinction here is from the other which is each one of the individuals.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof followed by an objection and answer. It first states the second research: God's knowledge encompasses all concepts—possible, necessary, and impossible—making it more general than power, which only applies to possibles. The proof is that the necessitator of knowledge is God's essence, while the requisite for being known is the essences and concepts of things; since God's essence relates equally to all, knowing some entails knowing all. Then an objection is raised concerning knowledge of the whole (all existents and non-existents): knowledge is either distinction itself or an attribute causing distinction, but there is no 'other' outside the whole to distinguish it from, so how can the whole be known? A proposed answer—that knowing the aggregate reduces to knowing individuals, so only individual distinction matters—is dismissed because if each individual is known, the aggregate is also known, and being known requires distinction. The correct answer is that distinction is needed only where there is another thing; when there is no other (as with the whole encompassing everything), distinction is not required. Here, the distinction is from each individual (the 'other' for the whole is each part).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that knowing the whole requires a separate act of distinction for the whole itself, as if the whole is an additional object beyond the individuals.
Correction: The correct view is that knowledge of all individuals suffices because the whole is not an additional object; distinction from each individual provides the necessary differentiation.

Checkpoint
How is the objection about knowledge of the whole (all existents and non-existents) answered?
A) The whole is known by a separate act of distinction.
B) Knowledge of individuals is impossible for God.
C) Distinction is not needed when there is no other; here the 'other' is each individual.
D) The whole is not an object of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of all individuals suffices for knowledge of the whole; no separate act of distinction is required because the whole is distinguished from its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge encompasses every single thought you have, even the ones you never act on? How does that change how you think about your private thoughts? Confront yourself: Is there a thought you would be ashamed for God to know? Now realize He already knows it. What will you do about that thought starting today?
?
The lesson says God's knowledge of all parts suffices for knowledge of the whole. Reflect: Do you tend to focus on the 'whole' of your life (goals, outcomes) while neglecting the 'parts' (daily actions, small sins)? How can you align your awareness of divine omniscience with the details of your day?
?
What does it mean that God's knowledge extends even to impossible things? Does this humble you or frighten you? How does it affect your trust in God's wisdom when you face impossible situations? Write down one impossible situation you are currently facing and pray to the One who knows it completely.

The Dahriyya Objection to God's Self-Knowledge and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the Dahriyya objection to divine self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Dahriyya argument that God cannot know Himself because knowledge is a relation, and the refutation based on knowledge as an attribute with conceptual distinctness.

Core Idea
God's self-knowledge is possible through conceptual distinctness, not real separation.

The Dahriyya (eternalists) raise a logical objection: if knowledge is a relation, it requires two distinct things, so God cannot know Himself. Al-Jurjani refutes this by explaining that knowledge is an attribute that entails relations, and conceptual distinction is sufficient for self-knowledge. This passage presents the objection and the full refutation, demonstrating a key move in Islamic theology regarding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
Understand the Dahriyya claim: knowledge is a relation, so it needs two distinct endpoints; self-relation is impossible.
The objection is based on the necessity of two distinct things for any relation.
2
Follow the initial refutation
Notice that the refutation denies knowledge is a pure relation; it is an attribute that has a relation to the known, and the attribute's relation to the essence is possible.
This frames knowledge as a real quality, not just a relation.
3
Trace the further objection and its answer
The opponent insists the attribute requires a relation between knower and known. The answer distinguishes two relations (knowledge-known, knowledge-knower), both possible.
This shows that the relation is mediated by the attribute, not direct between essence and essence.
4
Explore the concept of conceptual distinctness
The passage argues that distinction (tamayyuz) is not needed; rather, conceptual distinctness (i'tibar) between the essence as knowable and as knower suffices.
This is the key to resolving the problem without compromising divine simplicity.
5
Reflect on the human self-knowledge analogy
A knower can know themselves despite no real distinction, though humans are composite. The final answer uses the conceptual distinctness of the essence's two aspects.
The analogy shows the principle, then its limitation is addressed.

والمخالف في هذا الأصل أيضاً فرق ست: الأولى من قال من الدهرية: إنه لا يعلم نفسه لأن العلم نسبة والنسبة لا تكون إلا بين شيئين متغايرين هما طرفاها بالضرورة ونسبة الشيء إلى نفسه محال إذ لا تغاير هناك. والجواب منع كون العلم نسبة محضة بل هو صفة حقيقة ذات نسبة إلى المعلوم ونسبة الصفة إلى الذات ممكنة. فإن قيل: تلك الصفة تقتضي نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم فلا يجوز أن يكونا متحدين. قلنا: هي تقتضي نسبة بينها وبين المعلوم ونسبة أخرى بينها وبين العالم وهما ممكنتان كما الأحاد. الغير لم يكن هو بالمعلومية أولى وأما حيث لا غير فلا نسلم لزوم التميز، فإن التميز فرع تحقق المتميز عنه البتة، والحق في الجواب أن التميز هاهنا عن الغير الذي هو كل واحد من قوله: وهو أن الموجب للعلم ذاته إما بواسطة المعنى أعني العلم على ما هو رأي الصفاتية أو بدونها على ما هو رأي النفاة. قوله: والمقتضي للمعلومية ذوات المعلومات قيل: هذا يتوصف على إثبات كون الأشياء متساوية في صحة المعلومية ولعل المخالف لا يسلم ذلك. قوله: من قال من الدهرية المفهوم من سياق كلامه أن الدهرية يثبتون الواجب تعالى وأما ما سيذكره في النبوات من أن الدهرية على اختلاف أصنافهم ينفون القادر المختار فكأنه محمول على نفي القدرة والاختيار وإن كان بعيدا من المشهور. قوله: لأن العلم نسبة إلخ قيل: هذا إنما يدل على عدم العلم بالعلم لا بالذات كما تراه الفلاسفة ومتأخرو المعتزلة والجواب: إن المراد أن العلم للمعنى المصدري أعني الكشف والتميز نسبة لا يلائمه تقرير الجواب فتأمل. قوله: والجواب منع كون العلم إلخ هذا الجواب لا يناسب ما ذكره في آخر المقصد الأول من مقاصد العلم في موقف الأعراض وقد ذكرت هناك فلينظر فيه. قوله: قلنا: هي تقتضي إلخ قيل: العلم كما هو المختار صفة توجب تمييزاً وانكشافاً ولا شك في أن التميز والانكشاف نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم لا بين العلم وأحدهما، فيعود الإشكال ويحتاج إلى الجواب التسليمي الذي ذكره، وبالجملة ظهور المعلوم للعالم نسبة بينهما بالذات والعلم مرآه وآلة لتلك النسبة ونسبة الشيء إلى صاحبه أولى وأقدم من نسبته إلى آلته. قوله: ونسبة أخرى بينهما وبين العالم كون هذه النسبة نسبة أخرى لا يتوقف على تحقق التغاير بين العالم والمعلوم ولو بالاعتبار حتى يرد أن الكلام هاهنا مسوق على نفي اقتضاء ذلك التغاير لجواز أن يكون لشيء واحد بالنسبة إلى شيء آخر بعينهما نسبتان متغايرتان. فإن علم زيد بنفسه له نسبة إلى زيد بالقيام ونسبة أخرى إليه بالتعلق نعم بعد تحقق النسبتين حصل له محضة في المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث: في علمه تعالى، عرفت، وأما النسبة بين العالم والمعلوم فهي بعينها النسبة الأولى من هاتين المذكورتين اعتبرت بالعرض فيما بينهما فلا إشكال. سلمناه أي كون العلم نسبة محله ومتعلقه لكن لا نسلم أن الشيء لا ينسب إلى ذاته نسبة علمية بين فإن التغاير الاعتباري كاف لتحقق هذه النسبة. وكيف لا يكون كذلك وأحدنا يعلم نفسه مع عدم التغاير بالذات. لا يقال ذلك أي علمنا بذواتنا جائز لتركيب أنفسنا بوجه من الوجوه أي سواء كان تركيباً خارجياً أو ذهنياً. وكلامنا في الواحد الحقيقي الذي لا تكثر فيه أصلاً فلو كان عالماً بذاته لزم تحقق النسبة بين الشيء ونفسه قطعا بخلاف المركب إذ فيه كثرة يمكن أن يتصور بينها نسبة فلا يتجه النقض به. لأنا نقول: أحدنا على تقدير علمه بنفسه لو كان له نسبة إلى كل جزئية فقد حصل المطلوب إذ قد تحقق النسبة بينه وبين جميع أجزائه وهو عينه وإلا فلا يعلم إلا أحد جزأيه فيكون العالم غير المعلوم لأن الجزء غير الكل فلا يعلم نفسه والمفروض خلافه. فإن قلت من أين ثبت التغاير الاعتباري المصحح للنسبة؟ قلت: من حيث أن ذات الشيء باعتبار صلاحيتها للمعلومية في الجملة مغايرة لها باعتبار صلاحيتها للعالمية في الجملة وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفينا.
And the opponent in this principle also [has] six groups: The first is those who said from the Dahriyya: He does not know His own essence because knowledge is a relation, and a relation only occurs between two distinct things that are its two endpoints necessarily, and the relation of a thing to itself is impossible since there is no distinction there. The answer is to deny that knowledge is a pure relation, rather it is a real attribute having a relation to the known, and the relation of the attribute to the essence is possible. If it is said: That attribute necessitates a relation between the knower and the known, so it is not permissible that they be one. We say: It necessitates a relation between it and the known and another relation between it and the knower, and both are possible, like the individuals. The other is not more entitled to being known, but where there is no other, we do not concede the necessity of distinction, for distinction is a branch of the existence of that from which it is distinguished absolutely, and the truth in the answer is that distinction here is from the other that is each one of His saying: And that which necessitates knowledge is His essence, either by means of the meaning, i.e., knowledge, as is the opinion of the Attributes party, or without it, as is the opinion of the negators. His saying: And that which necessitates being known are the essences of the known things. It is said: This is based on establishing that things are equal in the soundness of being known, and perhaps the opponent does not concede that. His saying: Those who said from the Dahriyya. The understood from the context of his speech is that the Dahriyya affirm the Necessary Exalted, but as for what he will mention in prophethoods that the Dahriyya, despite their different types, deny the powerful Chooser, it is as if it is interpreted as negating power and choice, although that is far from the well-known. His saying: Because knowledge is a relation etc. It is said: This only indicates the absence of knowledge of knowledge, not of the essence, as the philosophers and later Mu'tazila see it, and the answer: The intended meaning is that knowledge in the verbal noun sense, i.e., uncovering and discrimination, is a relation, and the formulation of the answer is not suitable for it, so ponder. His saying: And the answer is to deny that knowledge is etc. This answer does not fit what he mentioned at the end of the first objective of the objectives of knowledge in the section on accidents, and I have mentioned it there, so let it be considered. His saying: We say: It necessitates etc. It is said: Knowledge, as is the chosen view, is an attribute that necessitates discrimination and uncovering, and there is no doubt that discrimination and uncovering is a relation between the knower and the known, not between knowledge and one of them, so the problem returns and needs the concessive answer that he mentioned, and in summary, the appearing of the known to the knower is a relation between them essentially, and knowledge is its mirror and instrument for that relation, and the relation of a thing to its owner is more primary and prior to its relation to its instrument. His saying: And another relation between them and the knower. That this relation is another relation does not depend on the existence of distinctness between the knower and the known, even by consideration, such that it could be objected that the speech here is directed at denying the necessitation of that distinctness because it is possible for a single thing, in relation to another thing exactly the same, to have two different relations. For the knowledge of Zayd of himself has a relation to Zayd in terms of subsistence and another relation to him in terms of attachment; indeed, after the two relations are realized, he has a pure [relation?] in the Fourth Observatory - Third Objective: On His Knowledge, Exalted, you have understood, and as for the relation between the knower and the known, it is exactly the first of these two mentioned relations considered accidentally between them, so there is no problem. We conceded it, i.e., that knowledge is a relation of its place and its object, but we do not concede that a thing cannot be related to its essence by a knowledge relation, for conceptual distinctness is sufficient for the realization of this relation. And how could it not be so? Since one of us knows himself without distinctness by essence. Let it not be said that, i.e., our knowledge of our essences is permissible due to the composition of our selves in some respects, i.e., whether the composition is external or mental, and our discussion is about the truly One who has no multiplicity at all, for if He were knowing of His essence, the realization of the relation between the thing and itself would necessarily follow, unlike the composite, for in it there is multiplicity such that a relation between them can be imagined, so the refutation by it is not valid. For we say: One of us, on the assumption of his knowledge of himself, if he had a relation to every part, then the desired is achieved, since the relation between him and all his parts, which is he himself, has been realized; otherwise, he only knows one of his parts, so the knower would be other than the known because the part is not the whole, so he does not know himself, contrary to the assumption. If you say: From where is the conceptual distinctness that justifies the relation established? I say: From the fact that the essence of a thing, in terms of its suitability for being known in general, is different from it in terms of its suitability for being a knower in general, and this amount of distinctness suffices us.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from the Dahriyya (eternalists) and its refutation, followed by extensive commentary. The Dahriyya argue that God cannot know Himself because knowledge is a relation requiring two distinct entities. The refutation denies that knowledge is a pure relation; it is an attribute that entails a relation, but the attribute's relation to the essence is possible. A further objection claims that the attribute implies a relation between knower and known, so they must be distinct. The answer distinguishes two relations: one between knowledge and the known, and another between knowledge and the knower, both possible. The commentary then discusses whether distinction (tamayyuz) is necessary, introducing the concept of conceptual distinctness (i'tibar). It uses the example of human self-knowledge: a human knows himself despite no real distinction, but since humans are composite, the objection that this doesn't apply to God's simplicity is addressed. The argument concludes that conceptual distinctness—the essence qua capable of being known versus qua capable of knowing—is sufficient for self-knowledge. The passage follows the order: objection, initial answer, further objection, answer, then detailed commentary on each part.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge is impossible because knowledge is a relation requiring two distinct things.
Correction: Knowledge is an attribute that allows self-relation through conceptual distinctness; the relation is not direct between essence and essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Dahriyya objection, why can't God know Himself?
A) Because knowledge requires a relation between two distinct things
B) Because God is too great to know Himself
C) Because knowledge implies ignorance of other things
D) Because God's essence is simple

Key Takeaway
God's self-knowledge is rationally possible through conceptual distinctness (i'tibar) between the essence as knower and as known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God knows Himself through a conceptual distinctness between His essence as knower and as known, what does that reveal about the depth of His self-awareness? How does this challenge you to be more honest about your own self-knowledge, especially the parts of yourself you avoid examining?
?
The Dahriyya rejected God's self-knowledge because they saw it as logically impossible. Are there areas in your faith where you have limited God based on your own logic? Will you allow this lesson to expand your understanding of divine transcendence, even if it means humbling your intellect?
?
Human self-knowledge, though imperfect, is a reflection of divine self-knowledge. How can you cultivate a deeper awareness of your own inner state (your intentions, fears, and motivations) starting today, so that you might draw nearer to the One who knows Himself and you completely?

The Ancient Philosophers' Objection That God Knows Nothing and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the second objection and refutation regarding divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient philosophers' argument that God knows nothing and the key refutation that distinguishes between actual and potential self-knowledge, avoiding infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowing something does not require actually knowing that you know.

The ancient philosophers raised a subtle objection: if God knew anything, He would have to know that He knows, leading to self-knowledge, which they deemed impossible. The refutation turns on a careful distinction about what knowledge of knowledge entails, showing that the regress argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the objection, the counter-objection about infinite regress, and the refutation.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection and counter-objection
Find the philosophers' claim that if God knows anything, He would know Himself, and the counter-objection that this leads to infinite regress.
3
Understand the refutation's core distinction
Notice how the refutation distinguishes between actually knowing that one knows and being able to know that one knows. The latter is sufficient and does not entail regress.
This distinction is the key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية من تلك الفرق من قال من قدماء الفلاسفة إنه لا يعلم شيئاً أصلاً وإلا علم نفسه إذ يعلم على تقدير كونه عالما بشيء أنه يعلمه وذلك يتضمن علمه بنفسه وقد بينا امتناعه في مذهب الفرقة الأولى. لا يقال: لا نسلم أن من علم شيئاً علم أنه عالم به وإلا لزم من العلم بشيء العلم بالعلم بذلك الشيء وهكذا فيلزم من العلم بشيء واحد العلم بأمور غير متناهية وهو محال. لأنا نقول: المدعى لزوم إمكان علمه به أي بأنه عالم وذلك مما لا خفاء فيه فإن من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم أنه صفات العالمية والمعلومية وتحقق التغاير الاعتباري لكن هذا التغاير متفرع على تلك النسبة لا سابق عليها كما مر في بحث العلم من موقف الأعراض، والمنفي هو اقتضاء سبق التغاير. قوله: اعتبرت بالعرض فيما بينهما فلا يلزم المغايرة بين العالم والمعلوم لأن معناه حينئذ تعلق علمه بالمعلوم فلا يقتضي إلا مغايرة العلم للمعلوم. قوله: فإن التغاير الاعتباري كاف إلخ قيل: التغاير الاعتباري إنما يكفي في تحقق النسبة بحسب الاعتبار لا بحسب نفس الأمر فلا يثبت كونه تعالى عالما بذاته في نفس الأمر بل بحسب الاعتبار فقط، والمقصود هو الأول وقد مر في بحث العلم جوابه فليتذكر. قوله: إذ قد تحقق النسبة بينه وبين جميع أجزائه إن أراد النسبة الواحدة نمنعها ولا يلزم من انتفائها عدم علمنا إلا أحد أجزائنا لجواز أن نعلم كل جزء بنسب متعددة وإن أراد مطلق النسبة لم يحصل المطلوب فليتأمل.
The second of those groups: those who said from the ancient philosophers that He knows nothing at all, otherwise He would know Himself, since on the supposition of His being knower of something, He knows that He knows it, and that includes knowledge of Himself, and we have shown its impossibility in the doctrine of the first group. It may not be said: We do not concede that whoever knows a thing knows that he is knowledgeable of it, otherwise knowledge of a thing would necessitate knowledge of knowledge of that thing, and so on, thus knowledge of one thing would necessitate knowledge of infinite things, which is impossible. For we say: The claim is the necessity of the possibility of His knowledge of it, i.e., that He is knowing, and that is something hidden from nothing, for whoever knows a thing can know that he [knows]. The attributes of being a knower and being known and the realization of conceptual distinctness, but this distinctness is branched from that relation, not prior to it, as passed in the discussion of knowledge in the section on accidents, and that which is negated is the necessitation of priority of distinctness. His saying: Considered accidentally between them, so there is no necessity of otherness between the knower and the known, because its meaning then is the attachment of His knowledge to the known, and it only necessitates the otherness of knowledge from the known. His saying: For conceptual distinctness is sufficient etc. It is said: Conceptual distinctness only suffices for the realization of the relation according to consideration, not according to actual fact, so it does not establish that He Exalted is knowing of His essence in actual fact, but only according to consideration, and the intended is the former, and its answer has passed in the discussion of knowledge, so let it be remembered. His saying: Since the relation between him and all his parts has been realized. If he intends a single relation, we deny it, and its absence does not necessitate that we do not know except one of our parts, because it is possible that we know each part by multiple relations; and if he intends absolute relation, the desired is not obtained, so ponder.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from ancient philosophers and its refutation. The philosophers argue that God knows nothing at all, because if He knew anything, He would know that He knows it, which implies self-knowledge, and self-knowledge was previously deemed impossible. The objection against this argument is that it leads to an infinite regress of knowledge about knowledge. The refutation answers that the claim is only about the possibility of knowing that one knows, not its actuality, so no infinite regress follows. The commentary then discusses that conceptual distinctness is sufficient for the relation, but it is not prior to the relation itself—a nuance from earlier in the text. It also addresses the human example: if a human knows all his parts, it is via multiple relations, not one single relation, so the objection about composition does not undermine the argument.

Correction: The refutation shows that only the possibility of self-knowledge is required, not actual infinite knowledge, so the regress is avoided.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that knowledge of an object does not entail actual knowledge of that knowledge, thus avoiding infinite regress and preserving the possibility of divine knowledge.

?

How does this lesson expose a subtle form of anthropomorphism in your own expectations of God's knowledge? What step will you take to correct this in your heart?
?
Consider the philosophical possibility that knowing something does not entail knowing that you know. In your own life, is there a truth about yourself that you know but avoid admitting that you know? What holds you back from full self-awareness before God?

God's Self-Knowledge: The Power of Conceptual Distinction
Al-Iji presents and refutes the objection to divine self-knowledge using conceptual distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of taghayyur i'tibari (conceptual distinction) allows us to affirm that God knows Himself without compromising His absolute oneness.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction makes self-knowledge possible even for the truly One.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial objection to God's self-knowledge: knowledge requires a relation between two distinct things, yet God is absolutely simple. Al-Iji introduces the notion of 'conceptual distinction' to show that a thing can relate to itself through different aspects, thus defending the doctrine of divine self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the passage from 'سلمناه' to 'يكفينا' carefully. Notice how the author states the opponent's view and then refutes it using conceptual distinction.
Pay attention to the phrase 'التغاير الاعتباري كاف'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says a thing cannot relate to itself in a knowledge-relation. Find where this is stated and how Al-Iji responds.
3
Understand conceptual distinction
Al-Iji argues that the essence's suitability for being known differs from its suitability for being a knower. This difference, though not real, is enough for the relation. Consider the example of self-knowledge in humans.
This is the key move in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محضة في المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : في علمه تعالى عرفت، وأما النسبة بين العالم والمعلوم فهي بعينها النسبة الأولى من هاتين المذكورتين اعتبرت بالعرض فيما بينهما فلا إشكال ( سلمناه ) أي كون العلم نسبة محله ومتعلقه لكن لا نسلم أن الشيء لا ينسب إلى ذاته نسبة علمية ) بین فإن التغاير الاعتباري كاف لتحقق هذه النسبة ( وكيف لا يكون كذلك ) وأحدنا يعلم نفسه ) مع عدم التغاير بالذات ( لا يقال ذلك ) أي علمنا بذواتنا جائز ( لتركيب أنفسنا بوجه من الوجوه ) أي سواء كان تركيباً خارجياً أو ذهنياً. (وكلامنا في الواحد الحقيقي الذي لا تكثر فيه أصلاً فلو كان عالماً بذاته لزم تحقق النسبة بين الشيء ونفسه قطعا بخلاف المركب إذ فيه كثرة يمكن أن يتصور بينها نسبة فلا يتجه النقض به ) لأنا نقول : أحدنا ) على تقدير علمه بنفسه ( لو كان له نسبة إلى كل جزئية فقد حصل المطلوب ( إذ قد تحقق النسبة بينه وبين جميع أجزائه وهو عينه ( وإلا فلا يعلم إلا أحد جزأيه فيكون العالم غير المعلوم لأن الجزء غير الكل ( فلا يعلم نفسه ) والمفروض خلافه فإن قلت من أين ثبت التغاير الاعتباري المصحح للنسبة ؟ قلت : من حيث أن ذات الشيء باعتبار صلاحيتها للمعلومية في الجملة مغايرة لها باعتبار صلاحيتها للعالمية في الجملة وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفينا . قوله : ( فإن التغاير الاعتباري كاف إِلخ قيل : التغاير الاعتباري إنما يكفي في تحقق النسبة بحسب الاعتبار لا بحسب نفس الأمر فلا يثبت كونه تعالى عالما بذاته في نفس الأمر بل بحسب الاعتبار فقط، والمقصود هو الأول وقد مر في بحث العلم جوابه فليتذكر. قوله : ( إذ قد تحقق النسبة بينه وبين جميع أجزائه إن أراد النسبة الواحدة نمنعها ولا يلزم من انتفائها عدم علمنا إلا أحد أجزائنا لجواز أن نعلم كل جزء بنسب متعددة وإن أراد مطلق النسبة لم يحصل المطلوب فليتأمل .
Pure. In the Fourth Observatory - Third Objective: Concerning His knowledge, exalted be He. It is known. As for the relation between the knower and the known, it is exactly the first relation from these two mentioned, considered accidentally between them, so there is no problem. (We granted it) i.e., that knowledge is a relation of its locus and its object, but we do not grant that a thing does not relate to itself with a knowledge-relation. For indeed conceptual distinction (al-taghayyur al-i'tibari) is sufficient for the realization of this relation. (And how could it not be so) while one of us knows himself, despite the absence of essential distinction? (It is not said that) i.e., our knowledge of our selves is permissible (due to the composition of our selves in some respect) i.e., whether externally or mentally. (And our speech is concerning the truly One in whom there is no multiplicity at all; so if He were knowing of Himself, the relation between a thing and itself would necessarily obtain, unlike the composite, for in it there is multiplicity among which a relation can be imagined, so the objection does not apply to it). For we say: One of us, on the assumption of his knowledge of himself, if he has a relation to every part, then the desired is obtained, for the relation between him and all his parts has been realized, and he is the same. Otherwise he knows only one of his parts, so the knower is other than the known, because the part is not the whole, so he does not know himself—contrary to the assumption. If you say: Whence is established the conceptual distinction that validates the relation? I say: In that the essence of a thing, in terms of its suitability for being known in general, is different from it in terms of its suitability for being a knower in general, and this amount of distinction suffices us. His saying: 'For conceptual distinction is sufficient etc.' It was said: Conceptual distinction suffices for the realization of the relation according to consideration, not according to actuality; thus it does not establish that He, exalted, is knowing of Himself in actuality, but only according to consideration, and the intended is the former. And its answer has passed in the discussion of knowledge, so let it be remembered. His saying: 'Since the relation between him and all his parts has been realized'—if he intends a single relation, we deny it, and its absence does not entail that we do not know except one of our parts, for it is possible that we know each part by multiple relations; and if he intends absolute relation, the desired is not obtained—so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God cannot know Himself. It begins with an established premise that the relation between knower and known is a relation of consideration. The author first states the opponent's position: knowledge is a relation between its locus and its object, and they argue that a thing cannot relate to itself in a knowledge-relation. The refutation uses the concept of conceptual distinction (taghayyur i'tibari): although the thing is one in essence, there is a distinction between its aspect as potentially known and its aspect as knowing. This suffices for a relation. The opponent objects that our self-knowledge is due to our composition, but God is simple. The response is that even in a composite being, the relation is not to the whole but to each part; if the relation is to all parts, it still entails multiplicity. The author then explains that the conceptual distinction arises from the different relational aspects of suitability for being known and for knowing. The commentator adds that this distinction is merely conceptual and not real, but the aim is to establish real knowledge. He also notes a nuance about the relation to parts: if a single relation is claimed, it is denied; if multiple relations, then the original point (that self-knowledge is impossible) is not achieved.

نسبة علمية
واحد حقيقي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge requires a real, essential distinction between knower and known.
Correction: Conceptual distinction (taghayyur i'tibari) is sufficient to ground a knowledge relation, as shown by self-knowledge in humans and argued for God.

Checkpoint

B) Conceptual distinction (taghayyur i'tibari)

Key Takeaway
Conceptual distinction allows a thing to relate to itself in knowledge, thus defending God's self-knowledge despite His absolute simplicity.

?

?
The opponent assumed that self-knowledge requires composition. What assumptions do you hold about God that might actually limit your understanding of His perfection? Are you willing to let go of those assumptions and embrace the deeper truth?

Refining the Argument: Objections on Composition and Reality
Al-Iji and the commentator address objections about the reality of conceptual distinction and the relation to parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuances in the argument about self-knowledge, including the distinction between conceptual and real distinction, and the objection about the relation to parts.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction is sufficient for real self-knowledge, not merely a mental exercise.

This lesson continues the discussion by addressing two important objections: first, that conceptual distinction only gives a relation in consideration, not in reality; second, that in composite beings, the relation to parts is problematic. Al-Iji and the commentator show that the argument holds with careful distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the additional passage
Read from 'قوله : ( فإن التغاير الاعتباري كاف إِلخ' to the end. This part contains the commentator's clarifications and a further objection.
Pay attention to the objection about conceptual distinction being merely mental.
2
Analyze the objection about reality
The objector says conceptual distinction only gives a relation according to consideration, not in actuality. The commentator refers to a previous answer. Consider why this objection is not fatal.
3
Examine the objection about parts
The second objection concerns the relation to parts: if a single relation is claimed, it is denied; if multiple relations, then the desired conclusion is not reached. Reflect on how Al-Iji's original argument already addressed this.
This shows the depth of dialectical reasoning in kalam.

Source Text

Pure. In the Fourth Observatory - Third Objective: Concerning His knowledge, exalted be He. It is known. As for the relation between the knower and the known, it is exactly the first relation from these two mentioned, considered accidentally between them, so there is no problem. (We granted it) i.e., that knowledge is a relation of its locus and its object, but we do not grant that a thing does not relate to itself with a knowledge-relation. For indeed conceptual distinction (al-taghayyur al-i'tibari) is sufficient for the realization of this relation. (And how could it not be so) while one of us knows himself, despite the absence of essential distinction? (It is not said that) i.e., our knowledge of our selves is permissible (due to the composition of our selves in some respect) i.e., whether externally or mentally. (And our speech is concerning the truly One in whom there is no multiplicity at all; so if He were knowing of Himself, the relation between a thing and itself would necessarily obtain, unlike the composite, for in it there is multiplicity among which a relation can be imagined, so the objection does not apply to it). For we say: One of us, on the assumption of his knowledge of himself, if he has a relation to every part, then the desired is obtained, for the relation between him and all his parts has been realized, and he is the same. Otherwise he knows only one of his parts, so the knower is other than the known, because the part is not the whole, so he does not know himself—contrary to the assumption. If you say: Whence is established the conceptual distinction that validates the relation? I say: In that the essence of a thing, in terms of its suitability for being known in general, is different from it in terms of its suitability for being a knower in general, and this amount of distinction suffices us. His saying: 'For conceptual distinction is sufficient etc.' It was said: Conceptual distinction suffices for the realization of the relation according to consideration, not according to actuality; thus it does not establish that He, exalted, is knowing of Himself in actuality, but only according to consideration, and the intended is the former. And its answer has passed in the discussion of knowledge, so let it be remembered. His saying: 'Since the relation between him and all his parts has been realized'—if he intends a single relation, we deny it, and its absence does not entail that we do not know except one of our parts, for it is possible that we know each part by multiple relations; and if he intends absolute relation, the desired is not obtained—so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God cannot know Himself. It begins with an established premise that the relation between knower and known is a relation of consideration. The author first states the opponent's position: knowledge is a relation between its locus and its object, and they argue that a thing cannot relate to itself in a knowledge-relation. The refutation uses the concept of conceptual distinction (taghayyur i'tibari): although the thing is one in essence, there is a distinction between its aspect as potentially known and its aspect as knowing. This suffices for a relation. The opponent objects that our self-knowledge is due to our composition, but God is simple. The response is that even in a composite being, the relation is not to the whole but to each part; if the relation is to all parts, it still entails multiplicity. The author then explains that the conceptual distinction arises from the different relational aspects of suitability for being known and for knowing. The commentator adds that this distinction is merely conceptual and not real, but the aim is to establish real knowledge. He also notes a nuance about the relation to parts: if a single relation is claimed, it is denied; if multiple relations, then the original point (that self-knowledge is impossible) is not achieved.

نسبة
نفس الأمر
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceptual distinction only provides a mental, not real, relation, so God's self-knowledge would be merely conceptual.
Correction: The author and commentator argue that conceptual distinction is sufficient for a real relation because the distinction is based on real aspects of the essence, not mere mental fabrication. The answer from the earlier discussion of knowledge clarifies this.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what is the limitation of conceptual distinction?
A) It only gives a relation according to consideration, not in actuality.
B) It requires a real distinction between knower and known.
C) It cannot apply to composite beings.
D) It leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Conceptual distinction, while merely mental in one sense, is grounded in real aspects of the essence and thus suffices for actual self-knowledge, as earlier discussions in kalam establish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your knowledge of Allah, is it merely conceptual—built from definitions and arguments—or does it reach the level of actuality? What would it take for your knowledge to be truly real, not just a mental consideration?
?
This lesson shows that conceptual distinctions, though mental, can be grounded in reality. In your own life, where do you settle for a merely notional understanding of your Creator, Creator's attributes, or your own relationship with Him? What practical step can you take today to move from 'i'tibar' to 'nafs al-amr' in that area?
?
If the deepest objections to God's self-knowledge are answered by careful reasoning, what does that demand of you when you encounter doubts about His attributes? Will you dismiss them lightly, or will you 'remember the answer from the discussion of knowledge'—meaning, will you actively recall and internalize the proofs you have learned?

Refuting the Claim that God Knows Nothing
Al-Iji presents and refutes the ancient philosophers' claim that God knows nothing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient philosophers' argument that God knows nothing and how al-Iji refutes it by showing that self-knowledge is not impossible for God.

Core Idea
The claim that God knows nothing fails because self-knowledge is not impossible.

This passage delves into an ancient objection to divine omniscience. The opponents argue that if God knew anything, He would have to know that He knows it, which they claim leads to an impossible self-knowledge. Al-Iji presents their reasoning and then systematically refutes it, highlighting a logical flaw in their assumption about self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim and its reasoning
The ancient philosophers claim that God knows nothing at all. Their reasoning: if He knew something, He would know that He knows it, which entails self-knowledge, which they deem impossible.
Notice that they assume self-knowledge is impossible for God.
2
Examine the objection of infinite regress
A potential objection arises: if knowing something required knowing that you know it, this would lead to an infinite chain of knowledge, which is impossible.
This objection is raised against the philosophers' implication, not against their conclusion.
3
Grasp the author's initial response
Al-Iji responds that what is claimed is the possibility of knowing that one knows, which is necessary for any knower. Denying this leads to sophistry.
He uses examples like knowing the Almagest to show that such self-knowledge is possible.
4
Understand the twofold refutation
If self-knowledge is impossible for God, the implication is denied because it only applies to those capable of self-knowledge. If self-knowledge is possible, then the consequent is not impossible.
Al-Iji also notes that the impossibility of self-knowledge has already been refuted.
5
Connect to broader theology
This lesson reinforces that divine omniscience is rationally necessary and that ancient arguments against it rely on flawed assumptions.
Reflect on why this matters for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( لا يقال : لا نسلم أن من علم شيئاً علم أنه عالم به وإلا لزم من العلم ( بشيء العلم بالعلم بذلك الشيء وهكذا فيلزم من العلم ( بشيء واحد العلم بأمور غير متناهية ) وهو محال ( لأنا نقول : المدعى لزوم إمكان علمه به ) أي بأنه عالم وذلك مما لا خفاء فيه ) فإن من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم أنه عالم به بالضرورة وإلا جاز أن يكون أحدنا عالماً بالمجسطي والمخروطات) وسائر العلوم الدقيقة الكثيرة المباحث المثبتة بالدلائل القطعية ) ولكن لا يمكنه أن يعلم أنه عالم به وإن التفت إلى ذلك وبالغ في الاجتهاد وذلك سفسطة) ظاهرة وإذا لزم الإمكان ثبت المدعى لأن إمكان المحال محال ) والجواب أنه إن امتنع منه تعالى علمه بنفسه منعنا الملازمة وقلنا الضرورة ) التي ذكرتموها إنما هي ( فيمن يمكنه العلم بنفسه وإن أمكن له علمه بنفسه (منعنا بطلان التالي ( المتضمن لهذا العلم الممكن بالفرض . ( وأيضاً قد مر بطلان ما ذكروه في ( إثبات (أنه لا يعلم نفسه.
The second group of those differing: those who said from the ancient philosophers: He does not know anything at all; otherwise He would know Himself, since if He were knowing of something, He would know that He knows it, and that entails His knowledge of Himself, and we have shown its impossibility in the doctrine of the first group. It is not said: We do not grant that whoever knows a thing knows that he is knowing of it, otherwise knowledge of a thing would entail knowledge of the knowledge of that thing, and so on, leading from knowledge of one thing to knowledge of infinite things—which is impossible. For we say: The claim is the necessity of the possibility of His knowledge of it, i.e., that He is knowing, and that is something obvious. For whoever knows a thing, it is possible for him to know that he is knowing of it by necessity; otherwise it would be possible that one of us know the Almagest and the Conics and the rest of the exact sciences with many topics established by definitive proofs, but cannot know that he is knowing of them, even if he reflects and exerts utmost effort—and that is manifest sophistry. And if possibility is necessary, the claim is established, because the possibility of what is impossible is impossible. And the answer: If knowledge of Himself is impossible for Him, we deny the implication and say: The necessity you mentioned is only for one who can know himself. And if self-knowledge is possible for Him, we deny the falsity of the consequent that includes this knowledge which is possible by hypothesis. Also, the falsity of what they mentioned in establishing that He does not know Himself has already passed.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the ancient philosophers that God knows nothing at all. Their reasoning: if He knew something, He would know that He knows it, which entails self-knowledge, which they hold impossible. The author then raises an objection against their reasoning: that this would lead to an infinite regress of knowledge. The author responds that what is claimed is the possibility of knowing that one knows, which is necessary for any knower; denying it leads to absurdity. The refutation is twofold: if self-knowledge is impossible for God, then the implication that knowing something requires knowing that one knows is denied because it only applies to those capable of self-knowledge. If self-knowledge is possible, then the consequent (that He knows Himself) is not impossible. The author also notes that the impossibility of self-knowledge has already been refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the infinite regress argument is a decisive refutation of the claim that knowing something requires knowing that one knows.
Correction: Al-Iji shows that the infinite regress is irrelevant because the issue is possibility, not actuality. The objection only applies if self-knowledge is impossible, which is not proven.

D) It confuses possible and necessary knowledge

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute of knowledge is established by reason and revelation; ancient arguments against it rely on unwarranted assumptions about self-knowledge.

?

?
The philosophers assumed self-knowledge is impossible for God because they measured Him by human limitations. Where in your own understanding of God have you unconsciously limited Him by your own experience? What does this lesson call you to correct?

Refuting Multiplicity in Divine Knowledge
Al-Iji refutes the claim that God knows only Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji refutes the claim that God's knowledge of multiple objects implies multiplicity in His essence by distinguishing between the essence of knowledge and its relational attachments.

Core Idea
Knowledge is one; only its relations multiply.

This passage presents a challenge: if God knows many things, doesn't that require many knowledges? Al-Iji clarifies that multiplicity is only in relations, not in the essence of knowledge itself. Let's examine the opponent's argument and the refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how the opponent's argument is presented and then answered.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that because knowledge of one thing is different from knowledge of another, God must have multiple knowledges, leading to infinite multiplicity in His essence.
3
Spot the key distinction
Al-Iji's answer hinges on distinguishing between the essence of knowledge (which is one) and its relations or attachments (which multiply).
Relations (idafat) are mere considerations, not real existents.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since relations are not real, multiplicity in them does not imply multiplicity in God's essence. Thus the objection is invalid.
This defends the unity of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : ( من الفرق المخالفة ( من قال : إنه لا يعلم غيره ) مع كونه عالما بذاته وذلك ) لأن العلم بالشيء غير العلم بغيره ) أي بغير ذلك الشيء من الأشياء الأخر ( وإلا فمن علم شيئا علم جميع الأشياء لأن العلم به حينئذ عن العلم بها وهو باطل، وإذا كان العلم بشيء مغايراً للعلم بشيء آخر ( فيكون له تعالى بحسب كل معلوم علم ) على حده فيكون في ذاته كثرة متحققة (غير متناهية هي العلوم بالمعلومات التي لا تتناهى وذلك محال بالتطبيق . ( والجواب : أنه ( أي ما ذكرتموه من كثرة العلم ( كثرة في الإضافات والتعلقات (و) ذلك لأنه لا نسلم تعدد ذات العلم بتعدد المعلومات بل (العلم واحد ) تتعدد تعلقاته بحسب معلوماته ( وذلك) أي تكثر الإضافات والتعلقات ( لا تمتنع لأنها أمور اعتبارية لا موجودة .
The third group of differing groups: those who said: He does not know other than Himself, while knowing His essence. That is because knowledge of a thing is other than knowledge of another thing, i.e., other than that thing from among other things. Otherwise, if one knows a thing, he would know all things, because knowledge of it would then be identical to knowledge of them—and that is false. And if knowledge of one thing is different from knowledge of another, then He, exalted, would have for each known object a separate knowledge, resulting in actual multiplicity in His essence, infinite, being the knowledges of infinite known objects—and that is impossible by comparison. And the answer: That which you mentioned of the multiplicity of knowledge is multiplicity in the relations and attachments. That is because we do not grant that the essence of knowledge is multiplied by the multiplicity of known objects; rather, knowledge is one, its attachments multiply according to its known objects. And that, i.e., the multiplicity of relations and attachments, is not impossible because they are things of consideration, not existent.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the third group's claim that God knows Himself but not others. Their argument: knowledge of one thing is distinct from knowledge of another, otherwise knowing one thing would entail knowing all things. This would imply multiple knowledges in God's essence, leading to an infinite multiplicity, which is impossible. The refutation: the multiplicity is not in the essence of knowledge itself but in its relations and attachments. Knowledge is one, but its attachments differ according to the known objects. Such relations are mere considerations (i'tibari), not real existent entities, so they do not imply multiplicity in God's essence.

Correction: Knowledge is one attribute; multiplicity lies only in relational considerations, not in the essence.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one; what multiplies are merely relational aspects, not real entities, so no multiplicity in the divine essence.

The Fourth Group's Argument: God Does Not Know the Infinite
Al-Iji presents the fourth group's argument that God cannot know the infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God cannot know the infinite because knowledge requires distinction, and why this leads to an infinite regress of knowledges.

Core Idea
If knowing requires distinguishing, then knowing the infinite would require infinite distinctions.

This passage introduces the fourth group's objection to divine omniscience. They argue that knowledge inherently involves distinction between the known and other things, and since the infinite cannot be fully distinguished in a finite manner, God's knowledge cannot encompass the infinite. Let us examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical connectors: 'since', 'because', 'and also'.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core premise
Find the phrase 'al-ma'qul mutamayyiz 'an ghayrihi' (the intelligible is distinguished from other than it). This is the key assumption.
3
Understand the consequence
The passage concludes that infinite objects would require infinite distinct acts of knowledge. Trace how this follows from the premise.
This leads to the same problem as the third group's argument.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider whether requiring distinction for knowledge is always true. Could God's knowledge transcend such limitations? The passage does not provide the refutation here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : ) من تلك الفرق . ( من قال : إنه لا يعقل غير المتناهي إذ المعقول متميز عن غيره ) لأن العلم إما نفس التميز أو صفة توجبه ولأنه لو لم يتميز عن غيره لم يكن هو بالمعقولية أولى منه . قوله : (إذ المعقول متميز عن غيره وأيضاً يلزم ثبوت العلوم الغير المتناهية المتعلقة بالمعلومات الغير المتناهية كما ذكرته الفرقة الثالثة .
The fourth group of those differing: those who said: He does not cognize the infinite, since the intelligible is distinguished from what is other than it. Because knowledge is either distinction itself or an attribute necessitating it, and if it were not distinguished from what is other than it, it would not be more entitled to intelligibility than it. His saying: 'Since the intelligible is distinguished from what is other than it' — also, it would entail the establishment of infinite knowledges related to infinite known objects, as mentioned by the third group.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the fourth group who claim that God cannot know the infinite. Their reasoning: the known (al-ma'qul) must be distinct from what is other than it, because knowledge is either distinction itself or an attribute that necessitates distinction. If a thing were not distinguished from others, it would not be more knowable. Therefore, each known object must be separately distinguished, leading to infinite knowledges for infinite objects. The commentator notes that this would lead to the same problem of infinite knowledges mentioned by the third group. The refutation is not given in this passage, but likely continues on.

The fourth group argues that God cannot know the infinite because knowledge requires distinction, which would entail infinite distinct knowledges.

Allah's Knowledge: One Essence, Multiple Relations
Refutation of the third opposing group on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that Allah's knowledge of multiple objects implies an infinite multiplicity in His essence by distinguishing between the essence of knowledge and its conceptual attachments.

Core Idea
Knowledge is one; multiplicity is only in conceptual relations.

Some theologians argued that if Allah knows Himself and also knows other things, His knowledge would have to be multiple, leading to an infinite regress. This passage presents their argument and the refutation, which hinges on a crucial distinction between the essence of knowledge and its relations to objects. Understanding this distinction is key to preserving divine simplicity while affirming comprehensive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the opponent's claim and argument, then the answer.
Pay attention to the terms 'taʿaddud dhāt al-ʿilm' and 'taʿalluqāt'.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
The opponent argues that knowledge of each distinct object must be a distinct entity, leading to infinite sciences in Allah. Pinpoint the steps of their argument.
Their conclusion: multiplicity in essence, which is impossible.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author responds by denying that the essence of knowledge multiplies. Instead, knowledge is one, and its attachments (taʿalluqāt) multiply conceptually. This avoids real multiplicity.
These attachments are 'umūr iʿtibāriyya' (conceptual matters), not real existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : ( من الفرق المخالفة ( من قال : إنه لا يعلم غيره مع كونه عالما بذاته وذلك لأن العلم بالشيء غير العلم بغيره أي بغير ذلك الشيء من الأشياء الأخر وإلا فمن علم شيئاً علم جميع الأشياء لأن العلم به حينئذ عن العلم بها وهو باطل، وإذا كان العلم بشيء مغايراً للعلم بشيء آخر فيكون له تعالى بحسب كل معلوم علم على حده فيكون في ذاته كثرة متحققة غير متناهية هي العلوم بالمعلومات التي لا تتناهى وذلك محال بالتطبيق. والجواب : أنه أي ما ذكرتموه من كثرة العلم كثرة في الإضافات والتعلقات وذلك لأنه لا نسلم تعدد ذات العلم بتعدد المعلومات بل العلم واحد تتعدد تعلقاته بحسب معلوماته وذلك أي تكثر الإضافات والتعلقات لا تمتنع لأنها أمور اعتبارية لا موجودة.
The third (of the opposing sects): those who say: He does not know other than Himself while being knowledgeable of Himself, because knowledge of a thing is other than knowledge of another thing—i.e., other than that thing among other things; otherwise, if one knows a thing he would know all things, because knowledge of it would then be knowledge of them, which is false. And if knowledge of a thing is different from knowledge of another thing, then there would be for Him—exalted—according to every known thing a distinct knowledge, so there would be in His essence an actual infinite multiplicity—the sciences of infinite known things—and that is impossible by comparison. The answer: what you mentioned of multiplicity of knowledge is multiplicity in relations and attachments, because we do not grant that the essence of knowledge multiplies with multiplicity of known things; rather, knowledge is one; its attachments multiply according to its objects. That—i.e., the multiplicity of relations and attachments—is not impossible because they are matters of consideration, not existent.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the third opposing view and its refutation. The opponents claim that if Allah knows Himself, He cannot know others because knowledge of one thing is distinct from knowledge of another. They argue that if knowledge were the same for all things, then knowing one thing would imply knowing all, which is false; so there must be distinct knowledges for each object, leading to an infinite multiplicity in Allah's essence—an impossibility. The author answers by distinguishing between the essence of knowledge and its attachments: knowledge is one in essence, but its relationships (tacalluqāt) multiply according to known objects. These relations are mere conceptual matters (umūr iʿtibāriyya), not real existents, so no multiplicity in the essence follows.

Key Terms
تعدد ذات العلم
تعلقات
أمور اعتبارية
غير متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the oneness of Allah's knowledge necessitates that He cannot know multiple distinct objects.
Correction: Knowledge is one in essence; multiplicity is only in the conceptual relations to objects, which does not compromise divine simplicity.

Checkpoint
How does the author avoid the conclusion of infinite multiplicity in Allah's knowledge?
A) By denying that Allah knows multiple things.
B) By asserting that knowledge is one and its relations are merely conceptual.
C) By claiming that the known objects are not distinct.
D) By saying the multiplicity is in the objects, not in the knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is one in essence; its multiplicity in relation to objects is conceptual, not real, preserving divine unity.
