Defending Divine Immutability: The Rejoinder on Knowledge of Non-Existence
Objection and rejoinder on change in divine knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rejoinder defends the Mu'tazila answer against the charge that divine knowledge would change when something transitions from non-existence to existence.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge of non-existence persists as knowledge of past non-existence.

The objection claims that knowledge of existence and non-existence are different, so God's knowledge must change. But the rejoinder cleverly shows that the original knowledge of non-existence does not cease; it simply becomes knowledge that the thing was non-existent. This preserves both God's omniscience and immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim that knowledge of non-existence and knowledge of existence are different, and why this would imply change.
Notice the example of Zayd being non-existent then existent.
2
Understand the rejoinder's distinction
The rejoinder says the first knowledge (that something will exist) includes knowledge of its present non-existence. After existence, this knowledge becomes knowledge that it was non-existent.
The key phrase is 'علم بأنه كان معدوماً'.
3
See how change is avoided
The original knowledge does not cease; it only changes its temporal reference. The second knowledge is new, but the first persists, so no alteration occurs.
This resolves the objection without admitting change in God.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
This defense upholds divine immutability while maintaining comprehensive knowledge of all temporal states.
This is a classic Ash'ari/Mu'tazila point of agreement on omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقد أجاب عنه مشايخ المعتزلة اعترض عليه بأنه إن سلم أن العلم بأنه وجد وسيوجد واحد لكن لا شك أن العلم بأنه معدوم والعلم بأنه موجود متغايران فإذا كان زيد معدوماً علم أنه معدوم وإذا وجد علم أنه موجود فيلزم التغير ولا يدفعه هذا الجواب وقد يجاب بأن العلم بأنه وجد والعلم بأنه سيوجد إذا كان واحدا فقيل : وجوده يعلم أنه معدوم ويعلم أنه سيوجد، فإذا وجد يعلم بالعلم الأول أنه كان معدوماً ويعلم بالعلم الثاني أنه موجود فلم يزل العلم بأنه معدوم بل علم بهذا العلم أنه كان معدوماً فأين يلزم التغير. والحق أن ما سيذكره الشارح بقوله وتوضيحه إلخ يشير إلى توجيه جواب المشايخ ويدفع عنه الاعتراض المذكور.
His statement: 'And the masters of the Mu'tazila answered'—an objection was raised against it that even if it is granted that the knowledge that it has existed and the knowledge that it will exist are one, yet there is no doubt that the knowledge that it is non-existent and the knowledge that it is existent are different. So if Zayd is non-existent, He knows that he is non-existent, and when he exists, He knows that he exists, so change follows, and this answer does not prevent it. And it may be answered that if the knowledge that it has existed and the knowledge that it will exist are one, then it is said: In its state of non-existence, He knows that it is non-existent and knows that it will exist. Then when it exists, He knows by the first knowledge that it was non-existent and knows by the second knowledge that it is existent. So the knowledge that it is non-existent did not cease; rather, by this knowledge He knows that it was non-existent. So where does change follow? And the truth is that what the commentator will mention with his statement 'and its clarification' etc. points to a direction of the answer of the masters and repels the aforementioned objection.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an objection to the Mu'tazila/Ash'ari answer and a rejoinder. The objector argues that even if knowledge of future and past existence are one, knowledge of non-existence and existence are clearly different. When Zayd is non-existent, God knows him as non-existent; when he exists, God knows him as existent. This seems to imply change. The rejoinder explains that the knowledge that something will exist (which includes its current non-existence) persists after existence as knowledge that it was non-existent. Meanwhile, a new knowledge of existence is added. The original knowledge does not cease; it merely becomes knowledge of past non-existence. Thus, no change occurs in the original knowledge. The passage concludes that the commentator's later clarification will further support this.

Correction: The rejoinder shows that knowledge of non-existence does not disappear; it becomes knowledge of past non-existence, so no change in God.

B) It remains as knowledge that it was non-existent

Divine knowledge of non-existence persists after existence as knowledge of past non-existence, preserving God's immutability while maintaining omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your existence never changed—He always knew you would exist and knew you as non-existent before—what does that say about your significance or insignificance in His eternal plan? How does that truth settle or unsettle your heart when you feel invisible or forgotten?
?
Your own knowledge changes daily: you forget, you learn, you regret. God's knowledge never changes. Are there areas in your life where you secretly wish God would 'forget' your past or 'learn' something new about your present? If He did, He would not be God. Knowing His knowledge is unchangeable, what is the one sin or doubt you need to stop hiding from His sight and instead trust His eternal knowledge of you?
?
The passage shows that God's knowledge of non-existence becomes knowledge of past non-existence. He never stops knowing what you were. But are you still living as if you are defined by your past non-existence (or past sin) rather than by His eternal knowledge that includes your future with Him? What decision do you need to make today to align your self-perception with how God knows you?

The Mu'tazila and Ash'ari Answer on Divine Knowledge Not Changing
Mu'tazila and Ash'ari argue that God's knowledge of existence and future existence is one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari argue that God's knowledge does not change with events, and how the concept of non-temporal knowledge resolves the apparent change.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not temporal, so past, present, and future do not apply to it.

In this passage, the author presents the response of Mu'tazila and Ash'ari scholars to the objection that divine knowledge would have to change as events unfold. They argue that because God is free from forgetfulness and His knowledge is eternal and non-temporal, His knowledge of an event before and after its occurrence is identical. The passage then draws on philosophical insights to explain that God's knowledge transcends time categories like past, present, and future.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally before analyzing.
Pay attention to the analogy about Zayd entering the city.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the key point: God's knowledge is continuous because He has no forgetfulness. Compare with human knowledge that requires renewal.
3
Understand the philosophical basis
The passage explains that God's knowledge is not temporal – it does not occur in time. Therefore, categories like past, present, and future do not apply, so no change occurs.
Consider the analogy with space: God's relation to all places is equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجاب عنه مشايخ المعتزلة وكثير من الأشاعرة بأن العلم بأنه وجد الشيء والعلم بأنه سيوجد واحد فإن من علم أن زيداً سيدخل البلد غداً فعند حصول الغد يعلم بهذا العلم أنه دخل البلد الآن إذا كان علمه هذا مستمراً بلا غفلة مزيلة له وإنما يحتاج أحدنا إلى علم آخر متجدد يعلم به أنه دخل الآن لطريان الغفلة الأول، والباري تعالى يمتنع عليه الغفلة فكان علمه بأنه وجد عين علمه بأنه سيوجد فلا يلزم من تغير المعلوم من عدم إلى وجود تغير في علمه وهذا الذي ذكروه مأخوذ من قول الحكماء : علمه تعالى ليس علماً زمانياً أي واقعاً في زمان كعلم أحدنا بالحوادث المختصة بأزمنة متعينة فإنه واقع في زمان مخصوص فما حدث منها في ذلك الزمان كان حاضرا عنده وما حدث قبله أو بعده كان ماضياً أو مستقبلاً، وأما علمه تعالى فلا اختصاص له بزمان أصلاً فلا يكون ثمة حال وماض ومستقبل فإن هذه صفات عارضة للزمان بالقياس إلى ما يختص بجزء منه إذ الحال معناه زمان حكمي هذا والماضي زمان هو قبل زمان حكمي هذا والمستقبل زمان هو ابعد زمان حكمي هذا فمن كان علمه أزلياً محيطاً بالزمان وغير محتاج في وجوده إليه وغير مختص بجزء معين من أجزائه لا يتصور في حقه حال ولا ماض ولا مستقبل فالله سبحانه وتعالى عالم عندهم بجميع الحوادث الجزئية وأزمنتها الواقعة هي فيها لا من حيث أن بعضها واقع الآن وبعضها في الماضي وبعضها في المستقبل فإن العلم بها من هذه الحيثية يتغير بل يعلمها علماً متعالياً عن الدخول تحت الأزمنة ثابتاً أبد الدهر، وتوضيحه أنه تعالى لما لم يكن مكانياً كانت نسبته إلى جميع الأمكنة على سواء فليس فيها بالقياس إليه قريب وبعيد ومتوسط
And the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila and many of the Ash'ari answered concerning it that the knowledge that it exists and the knowledge that it will exist are one, for whoever knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, when tomorrow arrives he knows by that same knowledge that he has entered the city now, if his knowledge is continuous without forgetfulness removing it. Each of us only needs another renewed knowledge by which he knows that he has entered now due to the occurrence of the first forgetfulness. But the Exalted Creator is impossible for forgetfulness, so His knowledge that it exists is exactly His knowledge that it will exist, and so change of the known from non-existence to existence does not necessitate change in His knowledge. And this which they mentioned is taken from the saying of the philosophers: His knowledge, exalted, is not temporal knowledge, i.e., occurring in time like the knowledge of any of us of events specific to particular times, for it occurs in a specific time, so what happened in that time was present to him, and what happened before or after it was past or future. As for His knowledge, exalted, it has no connection to time at all, so there is no present, past, or future. For these are attributes accidental to time in relation to what is specific to a part of it, since the present means this governing time, the past is the time before this governing time, and the future is the time after this governing time. So whoever's knowledge is eternal, encompassing time, not needing it for its existence, and not specific to any particular part of its parts, neither present, nor past, nor future is conceivable with respect to him. Thus God, glorified and exalted, is knower in their view of all particular events and their times in which they occur, not from the aspect that some of them are happening now, some in the past, and some in the future—for knowledge from this aspect changes—but He knows them with a knowledge exalted above entering into times, established forever. And its clarification: since He, exalted, is not spatial, His relation to all places is equal, so there is none with respect to Him near, far, or intermediate.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the answer of the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari scholars to an objection regarding divine knowledge and change. They argue that God's knowledge of a thing's existence and its future existence are identical because God is never subject to forgetfulness. Unlike humans, who need renewed knowledge when an event occurs due to forgetfulness, God's knowledge is continuous. Therefore, when a thing comes into existence, God's knowledge does not change—it remains the same knowledge He had of it before. This position is then supported by the philosophers' view that God's knowledge is not temporal. For a temporal knower, events are classified as past, present, or future relative to a reference point in time. But God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all times equally, so past, present, and future do not apply to Him. He knows all particular events and their times, but not under the aspect of 'now', 'past', or 'future', as that would imply change. Instead, His knowledge is transcendent and immutable. The passage concludes with an analogy: just as God's relation to all places is equal because He is not spatial, so His relation to all times is equal because He is not temporal.

Key Terms
علم زماني
غفلة
حال
ماض
مستقبل
علم أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge must change as events happen.
Correction: The passage argues that because God's knowledge is eternal and not temporal, it does not change. The change is only in the known object, not in the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage claim that God's knowledge of an event before and after it occurs is the same?
A) Because God uses a mental image that does not change.
B) Because God is not subject to forgetfulness, so His knowledge is continuous.
C) Because events are illusory.
D) Because God learns new things as they happen.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one, eternal, and unchanging because it is not subject to time or forgetfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of God's knowledge as if He 'finds out' about your actions after you do them, even if only in your heart? How does this lesson challenge that subtle assumption, and what will you do today to remember that He already knows everything without any before or after?
?
The passage says God's knowledge is not temporal—He knows all events, but not as 'now' or 'then'. When you worry about your future or regret your past, are you treating God's knowledge as if it were limited by time like yours? What specific worry or regret will you consciously surrender to God's eternal knowledge today?
?
If God's knowledge is one and unchanging, then His decree for your life is also one and fixed. Does this comfort you or trouble you? Honestly examine your heart: do you truly trust that His eternal knowledge of your entire life is better than your own changing plans? Write one area of your life where you need to submit to His unchanging knowledge right now.

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's Objection and the Response
Objection and response regarding divine knowledge of future vs past.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's objection that knowledge of future and past must differ is refuted by appealing to God's timeless knowledge.

Core Idea
An objection valid for temporal knowledge fails for God's timeless knowledge.

We now encounter an objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who argues that God's knowledge of a future event cannot be identical to His knowledge of its past occurrence because the objects themselves are different. The response that follows exposes a critical flaw: this reasoning only holds if knowledge is temporal. Since God's knowledge transcends time, the objection does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together. Notice how the objection is presented and then the response begins with 'قوله'.
The response is essentially a marginal comment refuting the objection.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the premise that 'will occur' is different in reality from 'has occurred'. This is the basis of the objection.
3
Understand the response's argument
The response says that for a non-temporal knower, these distinctions do not exist. Therefore, the objection is invalid.
The key is that God's knowledge is not bound by time.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the objection might seem plausible and why the response is effective. This clarifies the nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أنكر أبو الحسين البصري ذلك الذي ذكره هؤلاء المشايخ من أن علمه بأنه وجد عين علمه بأنه سيوجد. واحتج عليه بوجوه : الأول : حقيقة أنه سيقع غير حقيقة أنه وقع بالضرورة فالعلم به غير العلم به لأن اختلاف المتعلقين أي المعلومين يستدعي اختلاف العلم بهما. قوله: (الأول: حقيقة أنه سيقع غير حقيقة أنه وقع إلخ لايخفى أنه إذا أرجع كلام من الفلاسفة لم يتم هذا الاحتجاج إذ حقيقة سيقع، وحقيقة وقع، وإن كانا متغايرين إلا أنه لا يتحقق سيقع ووقع بالنسبة إلى من ليس علمه زمانيا ولا يتصور بالنسبة إليه ماض ومستقبل.
And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri denied that which these shaykhs mentioned, that His knowledge that it exists is exactly His knowledge that it will exist. And he argued against it with several proofs: First: The reality that it will occur is necessarily different from the reality that it has occurred, so knowledge of it is different from knowledge of it because the difference of the two objects, i.e., the two knowns, necessitates difference of the knowledge of them. His saying: (First: 'The reality that it will occur differs from the reality that it has occurred', etc. It is not hidden that if the statement of the philosophers is referred to, this argument is not established, for 'will occur' and 'has occurred', although they are different, yet 'will occur' and 'has occurred' are not realized with respect to one whose knowledge is not temporal, and past and future are not conceivable with respect to him.)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri to the earlier argument that God's knowledge of future existence is identical to His knowledge of past existence. His first proof is that the fact of something's future occurrence is necessarily different from the fact of its having occurred, so the knowledge of each is different because different objects require different knowledge. A marginal comment responds by noting that if one accepts the philosophers' view that God's knowledge is not temporal, then the objection fails. For a being whose knowledge is not temporal, there is no meaningful distinction between 'will occur' and 'has occurred'—past and future do not apply. Therefore, the objection is invalid when applied to God.

علم زماني
سيقع

متعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The response shows the objection only holds for temporal knowledge, not for God's timeless knowledge.

Checkpoint

B) By stating that the objection only applies to temporal knowledge, not divine knowledge.
C) By claiming that God does not know particulars.
D) By arguing that the objection is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The objection that knowledge of future and past must differ fails because God's knowledge is timeless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objection assumed that 'will occur' and 'has occurred' are separate. In your own life, which do you spend more mental energy on—regretting the past or fearing the future? If God is free from both, how can you imitate that freedom by trusting Him with both?
